Commonwealth v. Strube


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


274 Pa. Super. 199; 418 A.2d 365; 1979 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3490


March 19, 1979, Argued  
December 28, 1979, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 2105 October Term, 1978, Appeal from the Order and Judgment of Sentence in the Court of Common Pleas of Laneaster County, Criminal Division, No. 1230, 1979.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Lancaster County (Pennsylvania), which entered a judgment of conviction and sentence. Defendant claimed that the rape shield law, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3104(b), unconstitutionally violated his Fifth Amendment rights.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of rape and sentenced to a fine and concurrent terms of imprisonment, from which he appealed. The court found that the rape shield law, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3104(b), was constitutional and did not deprive defendant of his right to remain silent or assert a defense, that the trial court's explanation of "reasonable doubt" to the jury was proper, that it was proper to inform the jury of the location of the offense, and that the club's appellation "Sons of Satan" was not of such a lurid or morbid character to irretrievably prejudice the jury toward defendant, particularly in view of defendant's emphatic denial of membership. The judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The judgment of conviction and sentence were affirmed because the rape shield law did not deprive defendant of his constitutional right to remain silent or assert a defense.

 

614 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Martin


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


273 Pa. Super. 88; 416 A.2d 1102; 1979 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3388


July 31, 1979, Argued  
December 21, 1979, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 212 Special Transfer Docket, Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence Imposed on February 2, 1978 in the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County on Bill of Indictment Numbered 1996 of the May Term of 1977.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a jury verdict in the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which convicted him of voluntary manslaughter.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of voluntary manslaughter. On appeal, defendant alleged that the trial court abused its discretion by permitting the state to qualify the jury as to a capital case, when it was obvious that no aggravating circumstances were involved so as to justify treating the case as a potential capital case. Defendant also argued that the state committed reversible error in summation by stating a personal opinion as to witness credibility and that a statement he made to police should have been suppressed as the product of an illegal arrest. The court affirmed the conviction, holding that the jury could have found defendant guilty of first-degree murder, so it was acceptable to have treated the case as a potential capital murder case. The court also found that the state's remarks in summation were not error because they were meant to urge the jury to make a fair inference from the facts, and even if they were improper, they were harmless where defendant admitted the shooting but based his defense on other grounds. The court found the statements made to police were not the product of an illegal arrest because the police had probable cause to arrest defendant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction for voluntary manslaughter, because it was not reversible error for the state to make statements meant to urge the jury to make a fair inference from the facts. The court held that even if the statements were improper, they were harmless error where defendant admitted the shooting but based his defense on other grounds.

 

615 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Kempka


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


272 Pa. Super. 336; 415 A.2d 1226; 1979 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3329


March 23, 1979, Submitted  
December 7, 1979, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 2348 October Term, 1978, Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Monroe County, Pennsylvania, Criminal Division, at No. 317 of 1978


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Monroe County (Pennsylvania), which sentenced appellant under the minimum sentencing procedure established by 19 P.S. § 1057 (1964), and retained in part by the Sentencing Code, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1356(b)(Supp. 1978).


OVERVIEW: Appellant was sentenced under the minimum sentencing procedure established by 19 P.S. § 1057 (1964), and retained in part by the Sentencing Code, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1356(b)(Supp. 1978). Appellant challenged his minimum sentence, claiming it constituted a denial of due process. The court affirmed the decision of the lower court that sentenced appellant under the minimum sentencing procedure of the Sentencing Code because the legislature's grant of authority to the courts to establish a minimum sentence was not arbitrary and capricious. Moreover, both the ABA Project and Model Penal Code recommended the use of minimum sentences. The court reasoned that because the United States Supreme Court preferred minimum sentences to no-minimum sentences, that minimum sentences could not be unconstitutional. Finally, the court determined that appellant's claim that quasi-judicial officers, such as parole and probation professionals, were better trained to measure the rehabilitative needs of individual prisoners was better addressed to the legislature than to the courts.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the lower court that sentenced appellant under the minimum sentencing procedure of the Sentencing Code because the legislature's grant of authority to the courts to establish a minimum sentence was not arbitrary and capricious. The court held that appellant's minimum sentence was not unconstitutional because the United States Supreme Court preferred minimum sentences to no-minimum sentences.

 

616 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Farrar


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


271 Pa. Super. 434; 413 A.2d 1094; 1979 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3500


March 12, 1979, Argued  
October 26, 1979, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied May 6, 1980.


PRIOR HISTORY: No. 526 March Term 1977, Appeal from Order and Sentence in the Court of Common Pleas of Adams County, Criminal Division, CC-299-76 of March Term, 1977.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Adams County (Pennsylvania), sentencing her to a jail term rather than probation following a conviction for receiving stolen property pursuant to 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 3925 (1973).


OVERVIEW: Defendant, along with her husband and son, entered a home without permission, removed furniture, and moved with it to another state. Subsequently, a police officer conducted two warrant searches of defendant's home and seized several items of furniture. Defendant was convicted of receiving stolen property, pursuant to 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 3925 (1973). On review of the judgment of sentence, the court affirmed. The court found that the prohibition in § 3925 against retaining and disposing of stolen property made the offense ongoing and therefore, the trial court was not denied jurisdiction. The court found the searches were constitutional because the first item seized was very similar to that described in the warrant and the second item was within the officer's plain view. The court found that the judge's mention of the state's theory of the case in opening comments did not prejudice the defendant. The court found the judge's consideration of facts of a burglary in another state for which defendant was never charged and her bad influence on her son was proper as such facts were pertinent to the character of the defendant and the particular circumstances of the offense.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment sentencing defendant to a jail term rather than probation, following her conviction for receiving stolen property, because the court had jurisdiction of an ongoing offense, the searches of her home were valid, and appropriate facts considered before sentencing.

 

617 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Central Dauphin School Dist. v. American Casualty Co.


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


271 Pa. Super. 218; 412 A.2d 892; 1979 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3055


March 12, 1979, Argued  
October 19, 1979, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 229 March Term, 1978, Appeal from Judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphia County, Pennsylvania, at No. 1700 September Term, 1975.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant insurer challenged an order from the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County (Pennsylvania) that held, after reconsideration when respondent school district filed objections, that appellant was liable to refund monies after a defective tax resolution.


OVERVIEW: Respondent school district had an insurance policy from appellant insurer, which covered losses for wrongful acts. Respondent adopted a resolution, which imposed a tax on the occupations of all adult persons in the school district. The resolution was invalid and respondent had to return taxes paid. Respondent sought recovery for the liability from appellant. Appellant contended that respondent did not commit a "wrongful act" and did not sustain a "loss." The court held that the language of the insurance policy was written in broad terms and appellant was liable. Further, as respondent was legally obligated to pay the amounts demanded by exempt taxpayers, such payment was a loss under the policy. The court affirmed judgment of the trial court that appellant was liable.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in favor of respondent school district and held that appellant insurer was liable under the policy for the loss respondent incurred based on the defective tax resolution because the policy was written in broad terms to cover losses for "wrongful acts."

 

618 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Manlin


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


270 Pa. Super. 290; 411 A.2d 532; 1979 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3027


March 20, 1979, Argued  
October 5, 1979, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 2339 October Term, 1978, Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Lehigh County, Criminal Action, Nos. 868, 957 and 958 of 1977.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the judgment from the Court of Common Pleas of Lehigh County, Criminal Action (Pennsylvania) that convicted him of simple assault, two counts of criminal conspiracy, and three counts of official oppression under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 2701(a), 903(a), 5301.


OVERVIEW: Appellant challenged his conviction for simple assault, two counts of criminal conspiracy, and three counts of official oppression under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 2701(a), 903(a), 5301, for having assaulted, with other prison guards, three inmates during disciplinary proceedings, held in the prison of which appellant was then deputy warden. Appellant argued that § 5301 was unconstitutionally vague because the informations charged him with "mistreatment," and, since "mistreatment" was not defined in the code, he was not given fair notice that his conduct was forbidden. He also claimed the evidence was insufficient to support the criminal conspiracy convictions. The court found that appellant struck and kicked three inmates without justification or excuse during disciplinary hearings conducted by him in his official capacity. Appellant should reasonably have understood that his conduct was proscribed by the statutory prohibition of "mistreatment." The jury made the reasoned deduction from that evidence that appellant and the guards had an agreement or understanding among themselves to use physical violence against the prisoners during the hearings. The judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that convicted appellant of simple assault, two counts of criminal conspiracy, and three counts of official oppression because the statute under which he was charged was not unconstitutionally vague, and evidence showed appellant and the guards conspired to use physical violence against the prisoners during the disciplinary hearings.

 

619 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. West


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


270 Pa. Super. 301; 411 A.2d 537; 1979 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3043


June 4, 1979, Argued  
October 5, 1979, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied March 21, 1980.


PRIOR HISTORY: No. 2473 October Term, 1978, Appeal from Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Clinton County, Trial Division, Criminal Section, No. 25 April Term, 1975


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a decision from the Court of Common Pleas of Clinton County, Trial Division, Criminal Section, (Pennsylvania), which convicted him of unlawfully writing prescriptions in violation of the Controlled Substance Act, 35 P. S. § 780-113.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was initially charged with 54 counts of writing illegal prescriptions. At the time of trial, the prosecution chose to try appellant on only four counts. He was convicted of all four counts. On appeal, he argued that the undercover officer was wrongfully allowed to testify as to all of the prescriptions he obtained. The court held that while generally the commonwealth could not introduce evidence of other criminal activity, this was an exception to the general rule of inadmissibility. The evidence was admissible to show a common scheme or plan and it was necessary to show appellant's state of mind and good faith in writing the prescriptions. The court found the statute which proscribed writing illegal prescriptions was not vague and was easily understood. It also ruled that the absence of a particular voir dire question did not constitute ineffective assistance of counsel. Nor did counsel's failure to pursue an entrapment defense cause ineffective assistance; any error was rectified by the entrapment instruction. The court found that appellant was a licensed physician at the time the prescriptions were written and the decision was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision which convicted appellant doctor of writing illegal prescriptions because evidence of all the illegal prescriptions was admissible to show state of mind and a common scheme, counsel was not ineffective in voir dire, and the issuance of a license made him a practitioner within the statute.

 

620 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Sullivan


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


269 Pa. Super. 279; 409 A.2d 888; 1979 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2859


March 12, 1979, Argued  
September 7, 1979, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied November 26, 1979.


PRIOR HISTORY: No. 82 March Term, 1978, Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Adams County at No. CC-97-77.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Adams County (Pennsylvania), which convicted him of two counts of terroristic threats in violation of 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2706.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, defendant contended that the evidence was insufficient to support his conviction for terroristic threats. The court found that in an angry call to the State Police Barracks, defendant had threatened to kill a sheriff and that defendant had again threatened to kill the sheriff the next day during a coincidental encounter with the sheriff. The court determined that under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2706, defendant was guilty if he threatened to commit any crime of violence with the intent to terrorize. In looking at the legislative history, the court concluded that the purpose of § 2706 was to punish those who had the intent to terrorize another person and not those who only made threats in anger. Because there was no evidence defendant intended to carry out the threats he had made in anger, the court vacated the judgment based on insufficient evidence.


OUTCOME: The court vacated defendant's judgment for two counts of terroristic threats because there was no evidence defendant intended to carry out the threats he had made against a sheriff in anger as required to prove the offense.

 

621 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Thurmond


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


268 Pa. Super. 283; 407 A.2d 1357; 1979 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2663


March 23, 1979, Submitted  
July 27, 1979, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied November 26, 1979.


PRIOR HISTORY: No. 2416 October Term, 1978, Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas, Monroe County Branch - Criminal, No. 129 October Term, 1975.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a judgment of sentence entered by the Court of Common Pleas, Monroe County (Pennsylvania), which imposed a sentence of imprisonment and a fine while her co-defendant, on the same charges, received two years probation and a fine.


OVERVIEW: Defendant and co-defendant were convicted of gambling. Both were fined, but defendant was sentenced to a term of imprisonment, while co-defendant received probation. Defendant sought review. The court vacated sentence and remanded for a new sentencing hearing, at which the trial court was to place on the record its reasons for imposing different sentences for defendants. On remand the trial court imposed the same sentence, based on the facts that co-defendant had a serious heart condition and defendant lied in her testimony. On appeal, the court affirmed the trial court judgment holding that co-defendant's health was a valid consideration in imposing sentence. The court found that the trial court's consideration of the false testimony was justified where defendant's misstatements were willful and material, and the verdict necessarily established that she lied and was supported by substantial credible evidence. The court held that the trial court observed defendant's testimony and considered her false testimony only in light of other information relevant to its determination of a proper sentence. Defendant's sentence was thus not so harsh as to amount to an abuse of discretion.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court judgment that imposed a harsher sentence on defendant than her co-defendant. The court held that the trial court could consider co-defendant's heart condition in imposing sentence and defendant's false statements because they were willful and material, and because the verdict, which was supported by substantial evidence, established that she lied rather than that the jury did not believe her.

 

622 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Ashford


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


268 Pa. Super. 225; 407 A.2d 1328; 1979 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2639


December 8, 1978, Submitted  
July 27, 1979, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 1344 October Term, 1978, Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence in the Court of Common Pleas of Lancaster County, Criminal, No. 504 of 1977.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a judgment of sentence in the Court of Common Pleas of Lancaster County (Pennsylvania), where he was found guilty of the offense of terroristic threats.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was found guilty of the offense of terroristic threats. Following denial of his motions in arrest of judgment and for a new trial, he appealed, challenging the sufficiency of the evidence and the constitutionality of 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2706. The court found ample evidence to support the jury's conclusion that appellant made threats to arresting officers with the requisite intent to terrorize, and that his threats were more than mere spur-of-the-moment threats that resulted from anger. The fact that appellant was handcuffed and did not possess the means to immediately carry out his threats was irrelevant, because § 2706 did not require the present ability to inflict harm as an element of the offense. The claim that § 2706 was unconstitutionally vague was not presented in the lower court and thus was not preserved for appeal. The judgment of sentence was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court judgment of sentence for terroristic threats. There was sufficient evidence that appellant made threats to arresting officers with the requisite intent to terrorize, and because the present ability to inflict harm was not an element of the offense, the fact that appellant was handcuffed was irrelevant. Appellant failed to preserve the issue of the constitutionality of the statute on terroristic threats.

 

623 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Speck v. Finegold


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


268 Pa. Super. 342; 408 A.2d 496; 1979 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2614


April 14, 1977, Argued  
July 25, 1979, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Granted December 18, 1979.


PRIOR HISTORY: No. 7 April Term, 1977, Appeal from the Order Entered on July 21, 1976, of the Court of Common Places of Allegheny County, Pennsylvania, Civil Division at No. GD 76-07752


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, husband, wife, and infant, sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania) that dismissed appellants' action for wrongful life and wrongful birth.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, husband and wife, had two children born with a nerve disease. The husband had a vasectomy and was told he could engage in unprotected sex. After the wife became pregnant, appellants went to another doctor and had an abortion. Although the wife complained that she thought she was still pregnant, the doctor assured her that the abortion was successful. The wife then gave birth to a premature infant, who was stricken with the disease. Appellants sued for wrongful birth and brought suit on behalf of appellant infant for wrongful life. The trial court dismissed appellants' suit. The court affirmed the dismissal of the wrongful life claim. The court held the infant had no fundamental right to be born as a whole, functioning human being. The court held damages would be impossible to calculate, as the worth of never being born could not be ascertained. However, the court reversed the dismissal of the wrongful birth claim. The court held that there were viable claims of negligence and misrepresentations on the part of both doctors. The court denied emotional recovery, as there was no way to prove appellants suffered harm greater than parents of diseased children born naturally.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of the wrongful life action of appellants, husband, wife, and infant, because the infant had no fundamental right to be born as a whole, functioning human being. The court reversed the dismissal of the wrongful birth tort and assumpsit action, and remanded for trial on the merits, because there were viable claims of negligence and misrepresentations on the part of the doctors.

 

624 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Valentine


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


271 Pa. Super. 606; 419 A.2d 193; 1979 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2616



July 25, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] No. 1180 April Term, 1978, Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Somerset County at No. 106 Criminal 1977, Cirminal Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Somerset County (Pennsylvania), upon his conviction following a nonjury trial in which he was found guilty of arson, risking catastrophe, and a summary offense of criminal mischief. Before the court were defendant's motions for a new trial and in arrest of judgment.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was found guilty of arson, risking catastrophe, and a summary offense of criminal mischief upon evidence that he had fabricated and detonated an explosive device that damaged a county bridge. Defendant appealed from the judgment of sentence and filed motions for new trial and in arrest of judgment. The court denied both motions because defendant suffered no prejudice at trial, and the evidence was sufficient to support the fact-finder's conclusion that defendant's juvenile associate was recklessly placed in danger as prohibited by the arson statute. The court ordered, however, that defendant be sentenced only for arson and risking catastrophe because the offense of criminal mischief was merged into the offense of risking catastrophe, as all of the essential elements of the lesser offense were included in the greater.


OUTCOME: The court ordered that defendant's sentence for arson, risking catastrophe, and a summary offense of criminal mischief be revised to include only sentences for arson and risking catastrophe because the summary offense of criminal mischief was merged into his conviction for risking catastrophe, as both included the same essential elements. Finding no prejudice, the court denied defendant's motions for a new trial and in arrest of judgment.

 

625 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Boerner


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


268 Pa. Super. 168; 407 A.2d 883; 1979 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2619


October 23, 1978, Submitted  
July 18, 1979, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Granted October 26, 1979.


PRIOR HISTORY: No. 865 April Term, 1978, Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, of Allegheny County, at No. CC7704698A


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which convicted and sentenced him for driving under the influence of alcohol, under 75 P.S. § 3731(a)(1), in a nonjury trial. Appellant claimed that commonwealth erred in refusing to consider him for Accelerated Rehabilitative Disposition after it had first indicated that he was acceptable to the program and then cancelled the arrangements.


OVERVIEW: Appellant challenged his conviction and sentence for driving under the influence of alcohol, under 75 P.S. § 3731(a)(1). Appellant claimed that he was denied equal protection of the laws by being refused for consideration in the Accelerated Rehabilitative Disposition (ARD) program, that results from the breathalyzer machine were erroneously admitted, and that it was error to admit testimony regarding his intoxication. The court affirmed appellant's conviction, holding that appellant had no right to demand acceptance into ARD because under Pa. R. Crim. P. 175-185, whether a criminal defendant would be considered for ARD was wholly at the prosecution's discretion. The court found that it was error to admit the results from the breathalyzer machine because the health department had not yet certified the machine, but the error was harmless in light of the evidence of appellant's intoxication. The court held that the testimony of the officers as to appellant's observed state of intoxication was proper because intoxication was not a condition outside the realm of understanding or powers of observation of ordinary persons.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court judgment convicting and sentencing appellant for driving under the influence of alcohol. The court held that whether appellant was accepted into the Accelerated Rehabilitative Disposition program was wholly within the state's discretion. The court found that admission of uncertified breathalyzer machine results was harmless and that police testimony regarding appellant's state of intoxication was admissible.

 

626 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Bruno


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


268 Pa. Super. 15; 407 A.2d 413; 1979 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2548


May 8, 1979, Argued  
July 6, 1979, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied January 14, 1980.


PRIOR HISTORY: No. 18 Special Transfer Docket, Appeal from Conviction and Sentence of Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County, Criminal Division, No. 279 April Term, 1966, Criminal Actions Nos. 806 and 807


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the decision of the Court of Common Pleas, Montgomery County (Pennsylvania), which convicted defendant of murder in the first degree after the Pennsylvania Supreme Court reversed the result of defendant's first trial. Defendant alleged that the trial court erred when it refused admission of certain evidence, when it charged the jury, and when it refused to change the venue of the trial.


OVERVIEW: The outcome of defendant's first trial was reversed. Defendant challenged his conviction of murder in the first degree in a second trial. The court affirmed the conviction. The court first determined that evidence defendant sought to have admitted was properly excluded by the trial court because it did not bear on the elements of the M'Naughten test regarding defendant's claim of insanity or related to issues which were for the court to decide rather than the jury. The court also determined that the trial court had properly excluded or modified jury charges requested by defendant so that the charges accurately reflected the M'Naughten test. The court concluded that the trial court had no authority to consider the suppression of evidence that the Pennsylvania Supreme Court had determined, as part of the appeal from defendant's first trial, was admissible. The court found no abuse of discretion in the refusal of defendant's request for a change in venue after the trial court had taken reasonable steps to avoid any impact from pretrial publicity. Finally, the court noted that defendant had failed to properly preserve an issue related to alleged prosecutorial misconduct for review.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction was affirmed. The trial court did not abuse its discretion when it excluded evidence that did not bear on proof of the M'Naughten test, when it modified some of defendant's requested jury charge points to comply with the M'Naughten test, or when it denied defendant's motion for a change in venue after the trial court took reasonable steps to avoid any impact from pretrial publicity.

 

627 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Rebovich


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


267 Pa. Super. 254; 406 A.2d 791; 1979 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2507


September 15, 1978, Submitted  
June 22, 1979, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 1023 October Term, 1978, Appeal from a Denial of Motion for New Trial and/or in arrest of judgment after jury trial and from sentence of the Luzerne County Court, Criminal Division, filed to No. 2515 of 1976.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas, Luzerne County (Pennsylvania), which convicted him of failing to support a bastard child. Defendant alleged that 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 4323(a) was unconstitutional, that a mistrial should have been declared, and that the verdict was contrary to the weight of the evidence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged his conviction, under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 4323(a), for failing to support a bastard child. The court affirmed. The court concluded that the reference to "he" in the statute did not make the statute applicable only to males. Instead, the court found that the statute applied to either parent and was not, therefore, violative of Pa. Const. art. I, § 28. The court determined that the trial court did not err when it declined to declare a mistrial because of comments made by the Commonwealth during closing argument and made by the complainant during trial. In particular, the court found that defendant failed to lodge a timely objection to the Commonwealth's comments and that the trial court did not abuse its discretion when it refused to declare a mistrial as the result of the complainant's comments during her testimony. Finally, the court found that sufficient evidence existed to support the jury's verdict.


OUTCOME: The judgment convicting defendant for failing to support a bastard child was affirmed, because the statute under which defendant was convicted was not violative of the Equal Rights Amendment of the Pennsylvania Constitution, the trial court did not err when it refused to declare a mistrial, and sufficient evidence was adduced at trial to support the jury's verdict.

 

628 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Sojourner


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


268 Pa. Super. 488; 408 A.2d 1108; 1979 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2510


September 15, 1978, Argued  
June 22, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 2387 October Term, 1976, Appeal from Judgment of Sentence in the Court of Common Pleas, Trial Division, Criminal Section, Philadelphia County, at No. 2320 January Term, 1976.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas, Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which convicted defendant of simple possession and possession with intent to deliver heroin in violation of the Controlled Substance, Drug, Device, and Cosmetic Act, 35 P.S. §§ 780-113(a)(16) and (30) (1977).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of simple possession and possession with intent to deliver heroin in violation of the Controlled Substance, Drug, Device, and Cosmetic Act, 35 P.S. §§ 780-113(a)(16) and (30) (1977), and appealed. The court reversed, and state's petitioned for reconsideration was granted. The court modified its decision, as defendant was required to come forward with some evidence of authorization to possess a controlled substance before state needed to disprove authorization beyond a reasonable doubt. The court concluded that the issue of defendant's authorization was not supported by any evidence, thus the lower court was not required to submit it to the jury, and the charge that the jury could infer non-authorization from appellant's flight and discarding the heroin was not prejudicial to defendant. The court overruled Commonwealth v. Stawinsky, 234 Pa. Super. 308, 339 A.2d 91 (1975), that held that the burden of proving "authorization" by a preponderance of the evidence fell on the defendant.


OUTCOME: The judgment convicting defendant of simple possession and possession with intent to deliver heroin was reversed, but on reconsideration the court modified its decision, because defendant's failure to produce some evidence of authorization to possess the controlled substance precluded the issue's submission to the jury and charge that jury could infer non-authorization was not prejudicial.

 

629 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Hlavsa


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


266 Pa. Super. 602; 405 A.2d 1270; 1979 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2363


October 26, 1978, Submitted  
June 13, 1979, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 780 April Term 1978, Appeal from the order entered May 12, 1978 of the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, Crawford County, Pennsylvania at No. 585 of 1977.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Commonwealth challenged an order from the Court of Common Pleas of Crawford County (Pennsylvania), which order the results of a blood test performed on appellee to be suppressed in a criminal prosecution for driving while intoxicated. Appellant contended that the blood test was a lawful search incident to an arrest because appellee was unconscious in the emergency room of a hospital when the sample was taken.


OVERVIEW: Appellant prosecution challenged an order of a suppression court that suppressed the results of a blood test performed on appellee, who was charged with driving while intoxicated. The issue on appeal was whether appellee was under arrest at the time the blood test was performed, which would have made the blood test a lawful search incident to an arrest. A state police trooper ordered the blood to be taken while appellee was unconscious in the emergency room of a hospital. The appellate court noted that although appellee was unable to consent to the test, his consent would not have been necessary had he been placed under arrest. This was never done. The appellate court also noted that the officer never informed the hospital staff that appellee was under arrest and was not to leave the hospital upon regaining consciousness. Appellee was arrested 18 days after the blood sample was taken. The appellate court affirmed the order of the suppression court because no arrest was made before the blood sample was taken, and the taking of the blood sample was therefore not a search incident to arrest and was properly suppressed.


OUTCOME: The order of the lower court suppressing the results of the blood test was affirmed because the officer who ordered the blood test did not inform the hospital staff that appellee was under arrest, and therefore the taking of the sample was not a search incident to arrest. The court noted that appellee's consent would not have been necessary had he been placed under arrest.

 

630 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Ruff


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


266 Pa. Super. 497; 405 A.2d 929; 1979 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2334


May 8, 1979, Submitted  
June 1, 1979, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 51 Special Transfer Docket, Appeal from Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas, Philadelphia County, Trial Division, Criminal Section, September Term, 1976 at No. 1344.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction of murder of the third degree which was entered by the Court of Common Pleas, Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), contending that the court denied him due process by erroneously instructing the jury that evidence of voluntary intoxication could not serve to reduce murder of the third degree to manslaughter.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with murder. At trial, defendant entered evidence indicating that he was intoxicated at the time of the shooting. He objected to the court's instruction to the jury that evidence of voluntary intoxication could reduce murder of the first degree to murder of the third degree, but could not reduce murder of the third degree to manslaughter. A jury convicted defendant of murder of the third degree for killing the victim with a shotgun, and acquitted him of charges of murder of the first and second degrees and voluntary and involuntary manslaughter. Defendant appealed contending that the court denied him due process by erroneously instructing the jury that evidence of voluntary intoxication could not serve to reduce murder of the third degree to manslaughter. The court concluded that 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 308 prohibited an accused from entering evidence irrelevant to whether he acted out of malice,but Commonwealth still had to prove all elements of the offense charged, including state of mind, beyond a reasonable doubt. Defendant had offered no valid reason for striking down section 308 as unconstitutional, thus the conviction was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction of defendant of murder in the third degree because, though the relevant statute prohibited an accused from entering certain evidence irrelevant to whether he acted out of malice, Commonwealth was still required to prove all of the elements of the offense charged. The court also found that defendant had offered no valid reason the statute should be stricken as unconstitutional.

 

631 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth ex rel. Dimpter v. Kapp


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


266 Pa. Super. 429; 405 A.2d 509; 1979 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2283


December 5, 1978, Argued  
May 25, 1979, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 1384 October Term, 1978, Appeal from the Order dated March 21, 1978 and Opinion dated May 26, 1978 in the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County, Criminal Division, at No. 3638-A June Sessions, 1977.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Commonwealth sought review of the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County (Pennsylvania), which quashed the criminal complaint brought against appellee under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. 4323, for failure to support a bastard.


OVERVIEW: Appellant Commonwealth filed a criminal information against appellee under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. 4323, for neglect to support a bastard. Prior to indictment being found, the trial court dismissed the charges on the ground that the statute had been ruled unconstitutional. No appeal was taken. Subsequently, the constitutionality of § 4323 was upheld. Appellant then instituted a second criminal action against appellee pursuant to § 4323. The trial court granted appellee's motion and quashed the information on the ground that the doctrine of double jeopardy or collateral estoppel precluded appellee's prosecution on the second criminal complaint. On appeal the issue was whether either the doctrine of double jeopardy or collateral estoppel barred the second complaint. The court reversed, holding that appellee was not placed in jeopardy in the constitutional sense under the first criminal complaint because appellee's motion to quash the transcript was granted prior to jeopardy ever attaching. The court also held 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 109 was non-dispositive because the former prosecution neither resulted in an acquittal nor was it terminated after the indictment had been found.


OUTCOME: The court set aside the trial court's order which quashed the criminal complaint brought against appellee because neither the doctrine of double jeopardy nor collateral estoppel barred the second criminal complaint and the trial court erred in quashing the criminal complaint. The court reinstated appellant Commonwealth's second criminal complaint against appellee for failure to support a bastard.

 

632 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth ex rel. Platt v. Platt


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


266 Pa. Super. 276; 404 A.2d 410; 1979 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2245


December 4, 1978, Argued  
May 18, 1979, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied August 22, 1979. Petition for Allowance of Appeal Denied October 2, 1979.


PRIOR HISTORY: No. 1799 October Term, 1978, Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas Civil Division, Lancaster County, Commitment Docket No. 2, Page 309, Dated June 12, 1978.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant wife challenged an order entered by the Court of Common Pleas of Lancaster County (Pennsylvania), which committed appellant to a hospital for involuntary emergency psychiatric treatment under the Mental Health Procedures Act, 50 P.S. § 7303 (1976).


OVERVIEW: Appellant wife was involuntarily committed to a psychiatric hospital under a petition filed by her husband pursuant to the Mental Health Procedures Act, 50 P.S. § 7303 (1976). Appellant sought to bar the testimony of her husband and physician as privileged. On appeal, the court remanded the order. The court held both the husband and doctor could testify. It held that a spouse who testified at a mental health proceeding in "good faith" relative to the mental condition of his wife was not automatically placed into a position "adverse" to her and in fact was the strongest and most active protector of her best interests. The court believed appellant's physician and husband were the two people who were in the best position to detect and observe her mental disability. However, even though the record showed appellant posed a "clear and present danger" to others, the trial court erred in having appellant committed because the certificate attached to the petition did not satisfy the requirement that a physician needed to give the reasons as to why the involuntary treatment was necessary. Because it failed to do so, appellant was deprived of her right to question the physician.


OUTCOME: The order was remanded. Because the certificate attached to the petition did not provide a physician's statement with the reasons as to why the involuntary treatment was necessary, appellant wife was deprived of her right to question the physician. The court held that both appellant's husband and treating physician could testify at the involuntary commitment hearing.

 

633 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Wadzinski


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


266 Pa. Super. 56; 403 A.2d 91; 1979 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2172



May 4, 1979 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 734 October Term, 1975, Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, of Luzerne County, at No. 9390 of 1973.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant argued that 25 P.S. § 3234 was overbroad and vague in that it provided a criminal penalty for references to an opposing candidate and was not limited to types of advertisements. The Pennsylvania Supreme Court remanded the case to the court to determine whether the § 3234 was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellant argued that 25 P.S. § 3234 was overbroad and vague in that it provided a criminal penalty for references to an opposing candidate and was not limited to types of advertisements. Further, he called the provision a prior restraint of free expression in contravention of U.S. Const. amends. I and XIV. An equal protection argument was also raised, alleging that those candidates in urban areas with more ready and diverse forms of media coverage could give the required notice more expeditiously than those in rural areas who may have had available only periodic press coverage. The court considered that the goal of the statute was validly a part of the legislative prerogative under its police power. The court found it was a very limited restraint of free speech, applicable to those already and by their choice in the public arena as candidates. It was not unjust or unconstitutional to hold them to this duty of disclosure to their opposition, and to punish for violations thereof, when the end sought was clean, fair campaigning, a laudable goal for general conduct.


OUTCOME: The court held that the election code statute was constitutional because the goal to be attained by the statute was validly a part of the legislative prerogative under its police power.

 

634 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Bill Heard Leasing, Inc. v. Fineberg


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


265 Pa. Super. 129; 401 A.2d 834; 1979 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2036


October 25, 1978, Argued  
April 12, 1979, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 411 April Term, 1978, Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas, Civil Division, at No. 1590 October Term, 1971.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant judgment debtor challenged the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), that found him in contempt for the invocation of his U.S. Const. Amend V right against self-incrimination in an action by appellee creditor to determine appellant's assets in order to collect a judgment.


OVERVIEW: Appellant judgment debtor was noticed for deposition for the purpose of determining appellant's assets in order to collect a judgment. Appellant, on advice of counsel, refused to answer and invoked his U.S. Const. Amend. V right against self-incrimination. The trial court held a hearing and determined that appellant could not invoke his right against self-incrimination and held him in contempt. The court affirmed the order of the trial court because it found that the judgment debtor statute, 12 P.S. § 2243 (1967), provided transactional immunity to appellant for testimony that displaced his privilege against self-incrimination so that he could be compelled to testify.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court because it found that the judgment debtor statute provided transactional immunity to appellant for testimony that displaced his privilege against self-incrimination so that he could be compelled to testify.

 

635 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Possinger


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


264 Pa. Super. 332; 399 A.2d 1077; 1979 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1962


June 12, 1978, Submitted  
March 16, 1979, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 2074 October Term 1977, Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Trial Div., Criminal Sect., imposed on Bills of Information Nos. 1812 to 1818, May Term, 1976.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision of the Court of Common Pleas, Philadelphia County, (Pennsylvania), which convicted defendant of seven counts of feloniously dispensing a controlled substance 35 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 780-113(a)(14) (1977).


OVERVIEW: An undercover officer went to defendant's office, the officer told defendant a physician, that he wanted to go on a diet and asked for a prescription. After taking the officer's blood pressure, defendant asked him whether he had any allergies and which type of drug he preferred. The officer stated he wanted dexedrine, a controlled substance. Defendant wrote a prescription for 30 dexedrine. The officer returned to defendant's office on three more occasions each time receiving a prescription for dexedrine. Defendant gave two other officers prescriptions for a controlled substance without getting the officers' medical histories. Defendant was charged and convicted of violating 35 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 780-113(a)(14). Defendant sought review of the decision and the court affirmed. The court held that § 780-113(a)(14) was not unconstitutionally vague because the statute did not have to delineate the precise circumstances which constituted the bounds of the permissible practice of medicine. The court held that § 780-113(a)(14) gave defendant reasonable notice that his dispensing of medication without obtaining the patience's medical history, did not meet the requirements of the law.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for seven counts of feloniously dispensing a controlled substance because the applicable statute gave defendant reasonable notice that his conduct was prohibited. The court held that the applicable statute, which governed a physician's obligation when dispensing a controlled substance, was not unconstitutionally vague.

 

636 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Robinson


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


264 Pa. Super. 345; 399 A.2d 1084; 1979 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1965


June 12, 1978, Submitted  
March 16, 1979, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Granted June 1, 1979.


PRIOR HISTORY: No. 2500 October Term 1977, Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Trial Div., Philadelphia County, Imposed on Information No. 1068, January Session, 1977.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged his conviction for statutory rape by the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of statutory rape under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3102. The court found that defendant's representation to the victim that he was 21 and the testimony of defendant's sister established that he was 18 at the time of the offense. Her testimony was sufficient to establish his age because it was based on her positive knowledge and remembrance of the year and approximate date of his birth. The court found no constitutional invalidity in § 3102, because mistake as to the age of the victim has never been a constitutional defense to statutory rape. In addition, the court held that the clear terms of § 3102 represented the legislature's clear desire that, with respect to certain sexual offenses, a defendant could not prove his reasonable belief regarding the victim's age as a defense.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for statutory rape, having found that the evidence established that he was 18 years of age when the incident occurred. The court ruled that 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3102, was constitutionally valid and that it did not provide mistaken, reasonable belief regarding the victim's age as a defense.

 

637 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Hoffman


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


263 Pa. Super. 442; 398 A.2d 658; 1979 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1786


September 14, 1978, Argued  
January 26, 1979, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reargument Denied March 19, 1979.


PRIOR HISTORY: No. 1206 October Term, 1978, Appeal from the judgment of sentence by the Court of Common Pleas of Cumberland County, Criminal Division, No. 57 Criminal 1977.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the judgment of conviction of the Court of Common Pleas, Cumberland County (Pennsylvania), for theft by failure to make the required disposition of funds received as set forth in 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3927, and for the sale of unregistered securities pursuant to Pennsylvania Securities Act, 70 P.S. § 1-201 et seq. Post-verdict motions were filed and denied and defendant was sentenced to a prison term.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's corporation had been financed by investors who were general creditors of the corporation. Inevitably, bankruptcy resulted. The receiver found that no ledgers or books were kept, large sums of invested money were redirected to defendant's own use, and the corporation had issued additional unregistered promissory notes. Defendant was convicted of theft violations under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3927, and securities violations pursuant to Pennsylvania Securities Act (Securities Act), 70 P.S. § 1-201 et seq. On appeal, the judgment of sentence was affirmed. The court dismissed the contention that the definition of "security" in the Securities Act was unconstitutionally overbroad. The court found the statute clearly defined security to include a note. Constitutionally permissible definitions were severable from the infirm ones, if any, by Securities Act, 70 P.S. § 1-703(b). Also, failure to pinpoint the dates the investment monies were misappropriated was not fatal to the commonwealth's case because the evidence left no doubt that the money was misappropriated and the jury decided that defendant had accepted the money with the intent to misuse it.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment of sentence against defendant for violations of the Pennsylvania Securities Act and theft of investor's funds was affirmed. The court determined that the trial court's jury instructions were sufficient and proper, challenged voir dire questions proposed by defendant were properly excluded, the Securities Act was not unconstitutionally overbroad, and the evidence was sufficient to support the conviction.

 

638 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Dessus


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


262 Pa. Super. 443; 396 A.2d 1254; 1978 Pa. Super. LEXIS 4257


September 29, 1977, Submitted  
December 29, 1978, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Allowance of Appeal Granted August 13, 1979.


PRIOR HISTORY: No. 859 October Term, 1977, Appeal from Judgment of Sentence imposed on January 12, 1977, in the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division of Montgomery County, at No. 885 January Term, 1976.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County (Pennsylvania), following a conviction on four assault charges. Defendant claimed that the mandatory life sentence was unconstitutional, counsel was ineffective, the evidence was insufficient, and that a preliminary hearing before a non-attorney and errors in jury selection denied due process.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of assault with a deadly weapon while already serving a sentence of life imprisonment, and was given a mandatory sentence of life imprisonment. The court affirmed. The mandatory sentence did not violate the federal or state constitutions. The trial court did not err in permitting prior convictions to be used to impeach defense witnesses, or in ruling that a letter from the victim's personnel records was not an admissible business record. Counsel's failure to pursue certain discovery of prison records relating to the victim's records and to other prisoner assaults was not unreasonable, was harmless error at worst, and was not ineffective representation. The jury charge was not improper. A preliminary hearing that was held before a non-attorney did not deny due process because it was followed by a trial de novo before an attorney judge. The evidence was sufficient to support the judgment. Denial of individual juror voir dire was not an abuse of trial court discretion, nor was jury selection based on voter registration lists that did not systematically exclude any cognizable group of citizens.


OUTCOME: The convictions for assault were affirmed, and the mandatory life sentence was constitutional. The court further held that counsel was not ineffective and that defendant was not denied due process where the preliminary hearing was held before a non-attorney. The court also held that the evidence was sufficient to support the conviction, and that basing the jury panel on voter registration lists did not deny due process.

 

639 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


G. H. McShane Co. v. Travelers Indem. Co.


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


262 Pa. Super. 80; 396 A.2d 654; 1978 Pa. Super. LEXIS 4355


April 11, 1978, Argued  
December 28, 1978, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Nos. 310 and 475 April Term, 1978, Appeal from an Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Pennsylvania, Civil Division, entered September 30, 1977, and a Judgment of that court entered December 20, 1977, in Case No. G.D. 76-23714.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant insurance agency sought review of orders by the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which denied appellant's motion for judgment on the pleadings and granted appellee surety's judgment on the pleadings in a cause of action instituted by appellant against appellee for the amount of a consent judgment.


OVERVIEW: Appellant insurance agency filed a cause of action against a third party, an out of state resident, to recover premiums on policies of insurance which appellant had placed for the third party with various insurance companies. In order to obtain the release of property from an attachment which appellant obtained the third party filed a bond with appellee surety. Appellant obtained a consent judgment against the third party, which was never paid, and appellant instituted a second action against appellee for the amount owed under the consent judgment. The trial court's judgment granting appellee's judgment on the pleadings was affirmed because the court found that the Pennsylvania foreign attachment procedure had been declared unconstitutional. The court held that where a surety bond was executed to dissolve an attachment and where the attachment was in actuality prohibited by law, the surety bond was similarly void, unenforceable and uncollectible. The court further held that the ruling that held the foreign attachment statute unconstitutional was to be applied retrospectively because the decision did not establish a new principle of law.


OUTCOME: Trial court's judgment denying appellant insurance agency's judgment on the pleadings and granting appellee surety's judgment on the pleadings was affirmed because the Pennsylvania foreign attachment statute was void, thereby causing the surety bond to be similarly void, unenforceable, and uncollectable.

641 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Helser


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


261 Pa. Super. 378; 396 A.2d 447; 1978 Pa. Super. LEXIS 4510


March 13, 1978, Submitted  
December 28, 1978, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 443 March Term, 1977, Appeal from Judgment of Sentence Imposed by Court of Common Pleas of the 39th Judicial District of Pennsylvania, Criminal, Franklin County Branch, on July 27, 1977, to No. 238 of 1976.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant inmate sought review of a judgment of sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of the 39th Judicial District, Franklin County Branch (Pennsylvania) that was imposed upon a conviction for the offense of solicitation to commit murder.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of solicitation to commit murder of the husband of a woman with whom appellant had lived with for several weeks. Approximately, two hours after the jury had retired for deliberations on appellant's case, a tipstaff delivered a note to the trial court informing him that several members of the jury had information regarding appellant which was not introduced at trial. The jury was recalled and the trial court gave further instructions. Appellant's motion for a mistrial and request to voir dire the jury as to what information was referred to was denied. The court reversed the conviction and vacated the judgment of sentence because the possibility that prejudicial information reached the jury existed and the trial court did not take sufficient steps to deter the prejudicial effect. The court held that when there was a possibility of highly prejudicial materials reaching the jury, the trial court had to take appropriate protective action. Because the trial court was unaware of the nature of the information communicated to the jury, the required precautionary procedures should have included at least a determination of what information had been communicated.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the conviction of solicitation to commit murder, vacated the judgment of sentence, and remanded the case for a new trial because the court found a possibility that prejudicial information reached the jury and the trial court did not take sufficient steps to deter its possible prejudicial effect.

 

642 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Richbourg


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


260 Pa. Super. 438; 394 A.2d 1007; 1978 Pa. Super. LEXIS 4179


November 14, 1977, Submitted  
November 22, 1978, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 152 April Term, 1977, Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County, Criminal Division at Nos. CC7507572A and CC7600932.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant contested a decision from the Court of Common Pleas, Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which convicted him of robbery with a weapon, criminal conspiracy, carrying firearms without a license, and burglary. Appellant claimed that his constitutional rights were infringed by exclusion of blacks from the jury, that he should not have been directed to stand in court to be identified, and that the criminal conspiracy convictions were improper.


OVERVIEW: Appellant sought review of his convictions for robbery with a weapon, criminal conspiracy, carrying firearms without a license, and burglary, claiming that his constitutional rights were infringed because blacks were excluded from the jury, that he should not have been made to stand for identification purposes, and that the conspiracy convictions were improper under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 903. Appellant did not have a right to exactly proportional representation of races on a jury, but deliberate and systematic exclusion of members of a race would demonstrate a prima facie case of discrimination. The court found no evidence of systematic exclusion to prevent blacks from becoming members of the jury, and the record was bare of any facts from which a constitutional violation could be inferred. Having appellant stand was not prejudicial, and in-court identification of similar physical characteristics was permissible. The court did find that when a person conspired to commit a number of crimes, he was guilty of only one conspiracy so long as the crimes were the object of the same agreement, and vacated the conviction on one of the conspiracy counts and remanded for resentencing.


OUTCOME: The conviction of appellant on more than one conspiracy count was vacated as to one of the counts because, when a person conspired to commit a number of crimes, he was guilty of only one conspiracy so long as the crimes were the object of the same agreement. The court found no evidence of a systematic exclusion of blacks from the jury, and held that making appellant stand for identification was not prejudicial.

 

643 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Baggs


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


258 Pa. Super. 133; 392 A.2d 720; 1978 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3775


September 12, 1977, Submitted  
October 20, 1978, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 1233 October Term, 1977, Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Luzerne County to No. 30/76, Criminal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant father sought review from the judgment of sentence entered in the Court of Common Pleas of Luzerne County (Pennsylvania) for neglecting to support a bastard child in violation of 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 4323. Appellant claimed that his conviction was void because the statute was unconstitutional because it violated the Equal Rights Amendment of Pa. Const. art. I, § 28 and U. S. Const. amend. XIV, § 1.


OVERVIEW: Appellant father was charged and convicted of neglecting to support a bastard child in violation of 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 4323 (a). Appellant argued that the use of the pronoun "he" in the statute in issue made the statute applicable only to a male parent and, therefore, violated the state's equal rights amendment. Appellant also argued that the statute violated due process of law because it did not place a person on notice that he must contribute to the support of a child born out of wedlock and because it was unconstitutionally vague. The court rejected appellant's arguments and affirmed the judgment of sentence. The court pointed out that the rules of statutory construction applicable to statutes provided that words used in the masculine gender also included the feminine and neuter. The court also pointed out that the statute was not unconstitutionally vague simply because parentage was not determined until the trial for neglecting to support a bastard trial. The court noted that appellant had adequate notice of the charges against him.


OUTCOME: The judgment of sentence against appellant father for neglecting to support a bastard child was affirmed as statute requiring appellant to support the child was not unconstitutionally vague because use of "he" in statute requiring parents to support bastard children permitted its application to parents of both sexes, as statute spoke in terms of "parent" without distinguishing between parents on basis of sex; thus, statute was constitutional.

Commonwealth v. Bailey


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


258 Pa. Super. 364; 392 A.2d 836; 1978 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3776


June 23, 1977, Submitted  
October 20, 1978, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 2003 October Term, 1976, Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Trial Division, Criminal Section, at No. 885 December Term, 1970.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the order refusing to modify defendant's sentence to provide credit for time served prior to the conviction, which was entered by the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), contending that he was entitled to credit on the prison riot charge for time served.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested and charged with robbery, and he was released on bail pending trial. He was rearrested and had been in custody since that date. Defendant was charged with three counts of prison riots. Defendant was sentenced to five to ten years imprisonment on the original robbery conviction, and was subsequently convicted and sentenced on the homicide and prison riot charges. The homicide conviction was reversed by the supreme court, and the charge was nol prossed. Defendant was paroled on the robbery conviction, and began serving the two to five year term of imprisonment for prison riot. Defendant appealed contending that he was entitled to credit on the riot sentence for time served on the homicide charge. The court concluded that defendant was entitled to credit for time served on the prison riot convictions, thus the trial court's order was reversed and the case was remanded for further proceedings consistent with the court's opinion.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's sentence and remanded for further proceedings consistent with this opinion because defendant was entitled to credit on his conviction of prison riot charges when defendant was charged and held on other charges which were later dismissed.

Buchanan v. Century Federal Sav. & Loan Asso.


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


259 Pa. Super. 37; 393 A.2d 704; 1978 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3786


November 22, 1977, Argued  
October 20, 1978, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Nos. 962 and 963 April Term. 1976, Appeals from the Orders dated June 24, 1976, of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County at No. 2781 January Term, 1972.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant lending institutions appealed the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which disapproved a class action settlement, redefined the class, and imposed upon defendants' the cost of notifying the enlarged class in plaintiff mortgage borrowers' class action against defendants for receiving escrow payments for taxes and insurance without paying interest to plaintiffs.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff mortgage borrowers brought a class action against defendant lending institutions, claiming violation of trust, constructive trust, and implied contract because defendants did not pay interest on escrow payments for taxes and insurance. The trial court disapproved the proposed settlement, redefined the class, and imposed on the cost of notifying the enlarged class upon defendants. The court reversed the decision holding that the order disapproving the class settlement was appealable as a final order and appealable after it balanced the inconvenience and costs of piecemeal review and the danger of denying justice by the delay. In reviewing proposed class action settlements, the trial court had to conclude that the settlement secured an adequate advantage for the class in return for the surrender of litigation rights. The trial court was to analyze the settlement in terms of a range of reasonableness and not substitute its judgment for that of the parties. The court found that the trial court erred in concluding that two-percent interest on the loan was ridiculous, and, given the complexity of the litigation, the settlement was within the range of reasonableness.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the trial court to disapprove the class action settlement, holding that given the complexity of the litigation and the nature of the claims, a two percent interest settlement on the escrow payments was within the range of reasonableness and should have been approved by the trial court.

In re Sentry Secur., Inc.


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


259 Pa. Super. 385; 393 A.2d 880; 1978 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3813


March 29, 1978, Submitted  
October 20, 1978, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 1752 October Term, 1977, Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Chester County, No. 118, Mis., 1976, Crim., entered May 17, 1977, revoking the private detective license of Sentry Security, Inc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant corporation sought review of the revocation of its certificate of license to carry on as a private detective business by the Court of Common Pleas of Chester County (Pennsylvania). The commonwealth argued that appellant did not meet the necessary qualifications as a private detective under the Private Detective Act of 1953, Pa. Stat. 22 § 11 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Appellant corporation filed an application for a license to carry on a private detective business. Because the Private Detective Act of 1953 (the Act), Pa. Stat. 22 § 11 et seq., required an applicant corporation to demonstrate that at least one of its members had the qualifications to be a private detective, the application alleged that appellant's president had been employed as a detective for at least three years, and that as an employee at a police department, he had engaged in patrol and detective work. The trial court granted appellant a certificate of license. The district attorney petitioned to revoke the license as its investigation disclosed that appellant did not have the qualifications required. The state presented testimony from the township supervisor that appellant only handled minor juvenile disturbances and traffic accidents as a patrolman. The trial court revoked appellant's license. The court reversed the revocation concluding that the state failed to show that appellant's president was unqualified because appellant's criminal investigative work experience was the practical equivalent of regular employment as a detective over a sustained period of time.


OUTCOME: The trial court's order revoking appellant corporation's license was reversed because the state failed to show that appellant's president was unqualified as a private detective. The court found that the president's criminal investigative work was the practical equivalent of regular employment as a detective and that the president participated in many criminal investigations and performed investigative duties.

 

647 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Frisoli


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


255 Pa. Super. 593; 389 A.2d 136; 1978 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3050


March 21, 1977, Submitted  
July 12, 1978, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 2233 October Term, 1976, Appeal from Order of Restitution in the Court of Common Pleas of Lehigh County, Criminal, at No. 257 of 1974.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Lehigh County (Pennsylvania), which dismissed his P.C.H.A. petition. Appellant claimed ineffective assistance of counsel at his original trial and resentencing and averred that the trial court lacked jurisdiction to order restitution. Restitution was ordered after appellant pled guilty to aggravated assault and battery and assault with intent to maim for shooting his wife.


OVERVIEW: Appellant pled guilty to aggravated assault and battery and assault with intent to maim for shooting his wife. He was sentenced to a term in excess of that permitted by law and ordered to make restitution. Appellant filed a petition for writ of habeas corpus and was resentenced to a term that was authorized by the statutes in effect at the time the crimes were committed. Appellant was again ordered to make restitution. Appellant then filed a P.C.H.A. petition. He alleged ineffective assistance of counsel and claimed that the court lacked jurisdiction to order restitution. He appealed the dismissal of the petition. The court vacated the order of restitution. The court held that restitution could not be ordered without specific statutory authorization. When the crime was committed, restitution was limited to damages done to personal property, 18 P.S. § 5109 (1963); therefore, neither the trial court nor the resentencing court had the power to order appellant to pay restitution. The court did not reach appellant's ineffective assistance of counsel claim because the resentencing court's actions and the holding on restitution obviated the harm caused by counsel's conduct.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the order of restitution because neither the trial court nor the resentencing court had the power to order appellant to pay restitution. When appellant shot his wife, restitution was limited to damages done to personal property. The court did not reach appellant's ineffective assistance of counsel claims because the harm caused by counsel's conduct had been obviated.

 

648 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Stewart


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


257 Pa. Super. 334; 390 A.2d 1264; 1978 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3076


June 20, 1977, Argued  
July 12, 1978, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 293 October Term, 1977, Appeal from Judgment of Sentence of Court of Common Pleas of Lancaster County, Pennsylvania, Criminal Division, at No. 2475 of 1974.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged his conviction in the Court of Common Pleas of Lancaster County, Pennsylvania, on the grounds that his stop was unconstitutional and any evidence seized in connection therewith should have been suppressed, that his right to a speedy trial under Pa. R. Crim. P. 1100(a)(1) was denied, and that his prosecution constituted double jeopardy and violated the provisions of 18 P.S. § 110 (1972).


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed appellant's conviction for heroin possession and firearms violations and held that the evidence was admissible because the police obtained it in connection with a valid investigatory stop and a proper warrantless arrest. The court held that the officer's perception of him as a suspicious individual was reasonable in light of the facts the officer knew at the time and warranted his brief investigatory stop. The court found that his warrantless arrest was valid as it was based on probable cause. His trial commenced within the time required by Pa. R. Crim. P. 1100, after allowing for the extension the trial court properly granted on the state's timely motion, and therefore he was not denied his right to a speedy trial. The court held that he waived his right to complain about the convictions for the two offenses, arising from the same situation, because he did not request a consolidation. Appellant's failure to timely protest the separation of the two charges evidenced his apparent willingness to undergo separate trials for each offense and therefore his double jeopardy rights under U.S. Const. amend. V and 18 P.S. § 110 were not violated.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction because the situation warranted his brief investigatory stop, and his warrantless arrest was based on probable cause and was valid. He was not denied a speedy trial as his trial commenced within the time required. He waived his right to complain about undergoing separate trials for each of the two offenses, stemming from the same incident, by failing to timely protest the separation of his two charges.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Luzerne County (Pennsylvania), which denied appellant's petition seeking allowance to appeal from an adverse award of arbitrators without payment of costs and posting of bond.


OVERVIEW: The court quashed appellant's challenge from an adverse award of arbitrators without payment of costs and posting of bond. OUTCOME: The court quashed appellant's challenge from an adverse award of arbitrators without payment of costs and posting of bond. There was no infirmity of constitutional dimensions, and the order appealed from did not foreclose appellant from further proceeding in the Court of Common Pleas. By his own statement, appellant was not impoverished and therefore did not pursue the allowances provided by statute.
