Arnold v. Borbonus


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


257 Pa. Super. 110; 390 A.2d 271; 1978 Pa. Super. LEXIS 3122


April 12, 1978, Argued  
July 12, 1978, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 312 April Term, 1978, Appeal from the Order of October 4, 1977, of the Court of Common Pleas, Westmoreland County, at No. 3590 of 1976, Civil Action-Law.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant propane transmission company challenged a decision from the Court of Common Pleas of Westmoreland County (Pennsylvania), which sustained appellee employer's preliminary objections in the nature of a demurrer to its third party complaint.


OVERVIEW: Employee brought suit against appellant propane transmission company for injuries sustained in an employment related accident when the equipment he was using to perform excavation work punctured appellant's pipeline containing liquid propane. Appellant filed a third party complaint against appellee, employer of the injured employee. Appellee filed preliminary objections in the nature of a demurrer which stated that the Workmen's Compensation Act (Act), 77 P.S. § 101, et seq., barred appellant's third party complaint. The lower court sustained appellee's preliminary objections and dismissed appellee from the action. After reviewing the legislative history, the court concluded that the intention was to grant employer total immunity from third-party actions because the Act was a complete substitute for, not a supplement to, common law tort actions. It then held that the lower court properly sustained appellee's preliminary objections.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's decision which sustained appellee employer's preliminary objections to a third party complaint against it because the Workmen's Compensation Act granted employer total immunity from third-party actions as it was a complete substitute for common law tort actions.

 

651 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Gearhart


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


253 Pa. Super. 238; 384 A.2d 1321; 1978 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2574


April 12, 1977, Argued  
April 13, 1978, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 1005 April Term, 1976, Appeal From the Order Entered July 14, 1976, by the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, of Clearfield County at No. 34 March Term, 1973.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction from the Court of Common Pleas of Clearfield County, Criminal Division (Pennsylvania) for driving under the influence of alcohol. Defendant claimed the trial judge erred by giving a jury instruction regarding a presumption of intoxication based on a breathalyzer test result and the use of such evidence in establishing guilt beyond a reasonable doubt.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed his conviction of driving while under the influence of alcohol. On appeal, the court reversed the conviction, holding that the trial court erred by overstating the weight to be given the results of the breathalyzer test introduced into evidence. The court held that although the term "presumption" was used in 75 P.S. § 624.1, which permitted the introduction of scientific tests to show blood alcohol levels, the test results should only have been used to create an inference. The court held that the instructions given by the trial court did not highlight the non-compulsory nature of the "presumption" nor did it make clear the fact that the test results should have been considered together with the other evidence introduced.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction, holding that the trial court erred by overstating the weight to be given to the results of the breathalyzer test. The court held that the breathalyzer results only created an inference of intoxication rather than a presumption.

 

652 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Homison


SUPERIOR COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


253 Pa. Super. 486; 385 A.2d 443; 1978 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2580


April 15, 1975, Argued  
April 13, 1978, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 218 April Term, 1975, Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, of Butler County, at No. 76 June Term, 1971.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order of the Court of Common Pleasof Butler County, Pennsylvania, which dismissed his petition to expunge the record of defendant's conviction for selling drugs after he received a pardon from the Governor of Pennsylvania.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, a juvenile, was convicted of selling hashish. The Governor of Pennsylvania subsequently pardoned defendant. Defendant petitioned the trial court for an order to expunge the record of his conviction. The trial court dismissed defendant's petition on the grounds that defendant had not proved, or even alleged, that the pardon had been granted because of innocence. Defendant appealed and the court affirmed. While there was no case law on point, the court compared the issue to other cases where it was held that a conviction for which a pardon had been granted could be considered by a court in sentencing under a second offender statute; and where it was held that a previous conviction for which a defendant had been convicted and pardoned was admissible at trial in connection with the imposition of sentence.


OUTCOME: The dismissal of defendant's petition for an expunction of his conviction following a pardon was affirmed, because pardoned convictions could be used as evidence in criminal trials for other purposes.

 

653 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Jack v. Jack


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


253 Pa. Super. 538; 385 A.2d 469; 1978 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2583


November 10, 1976, Argued  
April 13, 1978, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 430 April Term 1976, Appeal from the Order of Dec. 19, 1975 of the Court of Common Pleas of Butler County, Pa., Civil Div. at A.D. No. 193 June Term, 1969.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant wife challenged an order of the Court of Common Pleas, Butler County (Pennsylvania) which directed payment of alimony pendente lite and additional counsel fees, and refused to direct payment of expenses. Appellee husband had filed an action for divorce and appellant was awarded alimony pendente lite and counsel fees. Appellant sought contempt for failure to pay alimony and counsel fees and expenses.


OVERVIEW: Appellant wife challenged an order refusing to award retroactive alimony from the date of the divorce decree and slashing attorney fees and costs. The court reversed the lower court's order and remanded the matter. The lower court's refusal to award alimony pendente lite from the date of the divorce decree because of the non-existence of a supersedeas was an error of law. The decree divorcing the parties did not terminate appellant husband's alimony obligation. The lower court should have ordered appellee to pay appellant alimony pendente lite retroactively to the date when he violated his continuing obligation. The lower court erroneously held that any fees and expenses incurred before the effective date of an amendment to 23 P. S. § 46 were not recoverable. The compensation provided for senior judges was irrelevant to an appraisal of the reasonableness of private counsel fees as was the amount of counsel fees allowed court-appointed counsel. The lower court should have considered both the value of counsel's services -- this should entail consideration of the result achieved by counsel, and of the time required to achieve it -- and the parties' respective financial situations.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the order of the lower court and remanded the case for the entry of a retroactive award of alimony pendente lite retroactive, and for consideration of the amount of counsel fees and expenses for appellant wife. The court instructed the trial court to take in to account appellee husband's ability to pay, appellant's necessity, and the extent of her separate estate.

 

654 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Funk


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


254 Pa. Super. 233; 385 A.2d 995; 1978 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2835


December 6, 1976, Submitted  
April 13, 1978, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of sentence by a Pennsylvania trial court for driving while under the influence of alcohol, 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 3731.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of driving while under the influence of alcohol, 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 3731, and challenged his conviction and sentence. He argued that the trial court's denial of his motion to suppress blood test results was erroneous. The court affirmed, holding that the police had probable cause to believe defendant was driving under the influence, as they smelled alcohol on his person, he was in a stupor, was losing consciousness, and was in an accident. The court found that the police properly took a blood test pursuant to 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 1547 to determine his blood alcoholic content. The court held that the state did not need to prove that defendant was unable to submit to a breathalyzer test; it was sufficient that the police believed he was unable to so submit, based on his precarious medical condition. Even though defendant did not knowingly and voluntarily consent to the test, the court held there was no error as the police acted reasonably in ordering the test. The test was constitutional as the police had probable cause, and as defendant had no constitutional right to refuse the test, he was not entitled to any Miranda warnings.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of sentence. The court held defendant's blood test was properly taken and properly admitted, as the police reasonably believed he was unable to take a breathalyzer test based on defendant's precarious medical condition. Because the police had probable cause, the search was constitutional, but because defendant had no constitutional right to refuse the test, he was not entitled to a Miranda warning.

Commonwealth v. Suplee


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


255 Pa. Super. 351; 387 A.2d 85; 1978 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2894


September 14, 1976, Argued  
April 13, 1978, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of sentence of the Delaware County Court of Common Pleas (Pennsylvania), which convicted him of violating the Pennsylvania Election Code, 25 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3234.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was the chairman of a committee to elect the Republican candidate for township supervisor. Supporters of the Democratic candidate ran an ad critical of the Republican candidate without prior notice to the Republican candidate that it would be run. In response, defendant authorized his committee to run an advertisement in reply without prior notice to the Democratic candidate. After the election, one of the Democratic candidate's supporters filed a private criminal complaint against defendant, alleging that he failed to notify the Democrats of the reply advertisement before it was published in violation of 25 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 3234. The lower court found defendant guilty, and he appealed the judgment. The court vacated defendant's sentence and held that the legislature did not intend the statute to be used to criminally prosecute a person for failing to notify the opposing candidate that a reply advertisement would be run in response to that candidate's advertisement.


OUTCOME: The court vacated defendant's judgment of sentence for failing to notify the opposing candidate that a reply advertisement would be run in response to that candidate's advertisement because the General Assembly did not intend the lower court to use the election code as a basis for criminal prosecution.

Kelsey-Barber Corp. v. Campbell

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant judgment creditor sought review of an order by the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County (Pennsylvania), which set aside a sheriff's sale of appellee debtor's home.


OVERVIEW: Appellee debtor cosigned a loan for their daughter and son-in-law. Ultimately a sheriff's sale of appellee's home resulted. The court determined that at the time of the judgment there was no statewide or applicable local rule in place by which to determine the sufficiency of appellant's notice to appellee. The court determined that the trial court had an inadequate basis for its conclusion, and therefore, the court vacated the order setting aside the sheriff's sale and remanded for further proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the order that set aside the sheriff's sale of appellee debtor's home, having determined that the trial court did not obtain enough information to discern whether appellee had sufficient notice of the sale.

Commonwealth v. Coder


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


252 Pa. Super. 508; 382 A.2d 131; 1977 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2890


June 15, 1976, Argued  
December 28, 1977, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 318 April Term, 1976, Appeal from Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Mercer County, Criminal Division, at No. 6 August Term, 1974.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Mercer County (Pennsylvania), which ordered appellant to pay $ 8,000 in court costs that were incurred as a result of the granting of appellant's motion for a change of venue.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was charged with wiretapping, conspiracy, and false swearing. The trial court granted appellant's motion for a change of venue due to excessive pre-trial publicity, and venue was transferred to a county 230 miles away. After appellant was convicted, the trial court charged him with $ 8,000 in costs for the travel, lodging, and miscellaneous expenses of the prosecution and necessary witnesses. The court reversed because the imposition of the costs incurred as the result of a change in venue would have acted as a penalty for appellant's seeking a fair trial, to which he was constitutionally entitled. The court remanded for a determination of what the costs would have been without a change of venue and for the imposition of those costs.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment that imposed $ 8,000 in court costs incurred as a result of the granting of appellant's motion for a change of venue because the imposition of such costs would have penalized appellant for asserting his constitutional right to a fair trial. The court remanded for determination of what the costs would have been without a change of venue.

Commonwealth v. McFarland


Superior Court of Pennsylvania

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of his conviction and sentence by the Court of Common Pleas of Luzerne County (Pennsylvania) for possession of a prohibited offensive weapon pursuant to 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 908.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was in the shower at his home when one of his friends brought out a sawed off shotgun to show the others. The gun was accidentally fired and killed one of the friends. Appellant was convicted of possession of an offensive weapon. Appellant contended that the statute under which he was convicted was unconstitutional and that his sentence was disproportionate to the crime. The court determined that the statute was not unconstitutional and did not deprive appellant of his due process rights because the burden of proof was upon the commonwealth; the part to which appellant objected was a defense only, to which appellant rightfully had the burden of proof. The court determined that the sentence was within the lawful limits and that the trial court had not abused its discretion. The court affirmed appellant's conviction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction and sentence for possession of a prohibited offensive weapon, having determined that appellant was not denied due process, that the trial court conducted the trial in a proper manner, and that sentencing was within the legal limit.

Commonwealth v. Black


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


251 Pa. Super. 539; 380 A.2d 911; 1977 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2900


June 22, 1976, Submitted  
December 2, 1977, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 650 October Term, 1976, Appeal from the order Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County, No. 2038 April Term, 1975, Criminal Section


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a conviction for retail theft entered in the Court of Common Pleas, Montgomery County (Pennsylvania), on the grounds that 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 3929(a)(1) and (a)(2), were unconstitutionally vague because they did not state whether criminal intent was required.


OVERVIEW: Appellant challenged his conviction for retail theft, asserting that 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. §§ 3929(a)(1) and (a)(2) were unconstitutionally vague because they did not specify whether criminal intent was required. Appellant took a tote bag from a shelf and removed the price tags. He went into the men's department and men's fitting room. When apprehended, a store detective found that appellant was wearing a new denim suit with the plastic from the price tag still on it, the manufacturer's ticket on the back of the jacket. Appellant clothes were found in the men's fitting room. At trial, appellant's defense was that his son died and the funeral was that day. On appeal, the court addressed appellant's legal arguments as ones of statutory construction. The court held that § 3929(a)(2) was not regulatory and did not require proof of criminal intent, but noted there would be no purpose in making the removal of store tickets unlawful without criminal intent. The court found that the jury instructions on criminal intent were unclear and vacated appellant's conviction on that basis.


OUTCOME: The court vacated appellant's conviction and sentence for retail theft, holding that the trial court's jury instructions regarding criminal intent were unclear and erroneous. The court remanded for new trial.

 

660 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Walton


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


252 Pa. Super. 54; 380 A.2d 1278; 1977 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2969


February 23, 1976, Submitted  
December 2, 1977, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 115 October Term, 1976, Appeal from Judgment of Sentence Imposed August 22, 1975 in the Court of Common Pleas, Trial Division, Criminal Section, County of Philadelphia, at No. 1716 June Term, 1975.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the Court of Common Pleas, County of Philadelphia (Pennsylvania), which convicted him of possession of an offensive weapon, a violation of 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 908. Defendant contended that the sword cane he possessed was not an offensive weapon, that the statute violated his due process rights under U.S. Const. amend. XIV, and that the commonwealth failed to prove an absence of a statutory defense.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed his conviction for possession of an offensive weapon, a violation of 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 908, contending that the sword cane he possessed was not an offensive weapon, that the statute violated his due process rights under U.S. Const. amend. XIV, and that the commonwealth failed to prove an absence of a statutory defense. The court affirmed the conviction, finding that the sword cane qualified as an offensive weapon under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 908(c) because it was capable of inflicting serious bodily injury that served no common lawful purpose. The court determined that the phrase "no common lawful purpose" was not unconstitutionally vague when read in conjunction with the rest of the statute concerning the sole purpose of the instrument being to inflict bodily injury. The court concluded that the commonwealth did not have to prove beyond a reasonable doubt the absence of the statutory defense in Pa. Cons. Stat. Ann. § 908(b) pertaining to possession of a curio or antique because the defense did not negate a material element of the offense, which required defendant to prove the exception to the offense.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction of defendant for possession of an offensive weapon, finding that the sword cane possessed was an offensive weapon, that the language of the statute was not unconstitutionally vague, and that the commonwealth was not obligated to prove the absence of a statutory defense as such was not an element of the offense charged.

Commonwealth v. Cluck


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


252 Pa. Super. 228; 381 A.2d 472; 1977 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2910


June 16, 1976, Submitted  
December 2, 1977, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 1712 October Term, 1975, Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence Imposed July 8, 1975, of the Court of Common Pleas of Centre County, Criminal, at No. 888 of 1974.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Centre County (Pennsylvania) that convicted appellant of violating the Controlled Substance, Drug, Device, and Cosmetic Act, 35 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 780-101 (1977). Specifically, appellant was found guilty of sale at retail of a nonproprietary drug, in violation of 35 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 780-113(a)(10).


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of violating the Controlled Substance, Drug, Device, and Cosmetic Act (Act), 35 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 780-101 (1977). In particular, the jury found appellant guilty of sale at retail of a nonproprietary drug, in violation of 35 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 780-113(a)(10). The appeals court affirmed, holding that the period of time that elapsed between the date of the offense and the date of appellant's arrest was not unreasonable and did not violate appellant's due process rights. The court also found that the testimony concerning an out-of-court photographic identification was not the type from which the jury could reasonably infer prior criminal conduct, and did not prejudice appellant. The commonwealth was not required to inform the jury that there was a promise to a principal witness of leniency in return for testimony against appellant, as appellant had the information and could have used it. The evidence supported the conviction and that the sale of the drug constituted a sale at retail. The Act clearly prohibited the causing of a sale at retail.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a judgment of the trial court that convicted appellant of violating the Controlled Substance, Drug, Device, and Cosmetic Act when it found appellant guilty of sale at retail of a nonproprietary drug, holding that the time elapsed between the offense and appellant's arrest did not violate appellant's due process rights. The evidence supported that appellant caused a sale of retail, as proscribed by the Act.

Commonwealth v. Rambo


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


250 Pa. Super. 314; 378 A.2d 953; 1977 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2572

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged by the prosecution with possession with intent to deliver a controlled substance and was convicted after a jury trial. Defendant appealed the judgment of sentence entered by the Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County (Pennsylvania) on grounds of insufficiency of evidence, the prosecution's failure to commence the trial with the required timeframe of Pa. R. Crim. P. 1100, and for other reasons.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with possession with intent to deliver a controlled substance and was convicted after a jury trial. Defendant appealed the trial court's judgment of sentence, and the court affirmed. The court held that the trial court correctly instructed the jury on drawing an inference of intent to deliver the controlled substance from the quantity of the hashish involved. Defendant's motion for a new trial based on after-discovered evidence was also properly denied by the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of sentence, holding that the prosecution presented sufficient evidence for the jury to find defendant guilty of possession with intent to deliver a controlled substance, that the trial was commenced during the required timeframe, and for other reasons.

Commonwealth v. Bey


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


249 Pa. Super. 185; 375 A.2d 1304; 1977 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1977


March 19, 1976, Submitted  
June 29, 1977, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 42 October Term, 1976

Appeal from the judgments of sentence of the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal Division, of Philadelphia County at Nos. 1390, 1391 September Session 1974.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which imposed concurrent sentences upon a finding of guilty of rape and involuntary deviate sexual intercourse. Defendant claimed that the trial court erred in certifying him as a juvenile to stand trial as an adult.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was a juvenile charged with rape and involuntary deviate sexual intercourse, was tried as an adult, was found guilty, and concurrent sentences were imposed. Defendant appealed the certification to stand trial as an adult and the judgment of sentence. The court found that the trial court erred when it failed to state its reasons for certification of defendant to stand trial as an adult, and that the statement violated due process requirements. The court found that none of the statutory methods of instituting juvenile proceedings was pursued by the law enforcement authorities prior to the appellant's detention, and held that after a juvenile was taken into custody, strict compliance with the provisions of the Juvenile Act by the law enforcement authorities obviated the need for any preliminary arraignment, and that the proper remedy was the suppression of prejudicial evidence obtained as a reasonable result of the unnecessary delay. The case was remanded for a new certification hearing.


OUTCOME: The certification of juvenile defendant to stand trial as an adult was remanded for a new certification hearing because the trial court's error in failing to state the criteria for the certification violated defendant's due process rights. If it was determined that certification was proper, then the judgment of sentence was affirmed.

OVERVIEW: Appellant brought her 28-month-old son to a hospital, where it was discovered that he had a skin disease, untreated injuries, poor personal hygiene, and communication skills not commensurate with his chronological age. Commonwealth v. Love


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


248 Pa. Super. 387; 375 A.2d 151; 1977 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2021


June 22, 1976, Submitted  
June 29, 1977, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] NO. 666 OCTOBER TERM, 1976

Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence of the Court of Common Pleas, Trial Division, Criminal Section of Philadelphia County, at No. 521 July Term, 1974.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which issued a judgment of sentence against him for the offense of criminal conspiracy.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted by a jury on a charge of criminal conspiracy. On appeal the court affirmed, holding that there was clearly sufficient evidence to prove the crime of criminal conspiracy. This evidence included testimony from two eyewitnesses as well as a pre-trial inculpatory statement made by appellant to the police that appellant was part of a gang of youths that plotted to ambush youths from a rival gang as the latter passed through the neighborhood of the former. The evidence also included appellant's admission that he shouted encouragement to his armed companion to continue shooting the victim, and a witness's testimony that he saw appellant pointing a gun at him immediately after the deceased victim was shot. The court also held that as long as the evidence was clearly sufficient, an inconsistent verdict was not grounds for granting a new trial. Finally, the court found no abuse of discretion in the trial court's ruling that gave appellee commonwealth permission to impeach appellant with evidence of a prior conviction. The trial court considered all the requisite factors on the record in making its ruling.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of sentence issued against appellant. The court held that there was clearly sufficient evidence to prove the crime of criminal conspiracy, and that as long as the evidence was clearly sufficient, an inconsistent verdict was not grounds for granting a new trial. The court also found no error in the trial court's ruling that permitted appellee commonwealth to impeach appellant through evidence of a prior conviction.

 

668 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Hellman v. Hellman


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


246 Pa. Super. 536; 371 A.2d 964; 1977 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1650


December 10, 1976, Argued  
March 31, 1977, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 1214 October Term, 1975

Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Lehigh County, Criminal Division, at No. 368-A of 1972.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant wife sought review from the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Lehigh County (Pennsylvania), which found in favor of appellee husband and terminated an order of support after finding that appellant had committed adultery.


OVERVIEW: After appellee husband learned of appellant wife's adultery, appellee petitioned the court to reduce the amount of spousal support for appellant. The court terminated the order of support, finding appellant had committed adultery. Appellant sought review. The court held that the lower court abused its discretion by refusing to consider evidence of the husband's misconduct, and remanded the case to the trial court. The court concluded that evidence of a husband's misconduct was relevant to a proceeding to vacate a wife's support on the basis of her adultery. Although an act of adultery on the part of the husband did not give a wife a right to act forever in a licentious manner and still command support from her husband, the court held that support laws were not promulgated for the purpose of rewarding a wife's good behavior. The court held that a court should not balance the parties' misdeeds against each other, but a court must consider evidence of a husband's misconduct before it could properly exercise its discretion.


OUTCOME: The court held that the lower court abused its discretion by refusing to consider evidence of appellee husband's misconduct before terminating an order of support for appellant wife. The court remanded the matter to the trial court with a procedendo.

 

669 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Kindness


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


247 Pa. Super. 99; 371 A.2d 1346; 1977 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1586


March 8, 1976, Argued  
March 31, 1977, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 124 March Term, 1976

Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence Imposed by the Court of Common Pleas, Criminal, of Dauphin County at No. 1727 of 1974.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County, Criminal Division, Pennsylvania, which imposed sentence after he was convicted after a nonjury trial of driving under the influence of intoxicating liquor.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with driving under the influence of intoxicating liquor. After defendant was arraigned, he filed a motion to submit his case for consideration under the Accelerated Rehabilitative Disposition Program (ARD), which provided for suspension of criminal proceedings before conviction on the condition that an accused fulfill a court-ordered condition. The district attorney declined to submit the case, defendant was ultimately convicted, and the trial court entered a judgment of sentence. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that a prosecutorial decision not to move a case for ARD was not an improper delegation of sentencing power because the trial court was not prevented from placing a defendant on probation in the event of a guilty verdict. The court held that the prosecutorial policy as to exclusion of drunk drivers from ARD did not amount to a denial of equal protection because the law, as administered, dealt alike with all members of the class. The court held that exclusion of drunk drivers from ARD in Dauphin County, but not in others, did not result in a denial of equal protection.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment of sentence imposed after defendant was convicted of driving under the influence of intoxicating liquor because the denial by the commonwealth of defendant's participation in the Accelerated Rehabilitative Disposition Program did not violate his constitutional rights to equal protection or constitute an improper delegation of sentencing power.

 

670 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Meyer


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


247 Pa. Super. 326; 372 A.2d 850; 1977 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1596


September 16, 1976, Argued  
March 31, 1977, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 1656 October TErm, 1976

Appeal from the Order of the Court of President Judge Dale F. Shughart, Court of Common Pleas of (Criminal) Cumberland County, dated May 4, 1976, Sustaining the Defendant's Application for Pre-trial Relief. (No. 280 of 1975)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Commonwealth sought review of a decision of the Court of Common Pleas (Pennsylvania), which entered an order granting defendant's motion for suppression of evidence in a prosecution for driving under the influence of alcohol.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested for driving under the influence after he exhibited indications of intoxication at the scene of an accident, although he was not in the vehicle at the time the officers arrived. The trial court suppressed a pre-arrest statement made by defendant prior to being advised of his constitutional rights and post-arrest evidence because it was obtained pursuant to an unlawful arrest. The trial court found the arrest was unlawful because the misdemeanor did not occur in the officer's presence and was made without a warrant, in violation of Pa. R. Crim. P. 101, even though the arrest was possibly lawful under 75 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 1204(a) (1976). The trial court found that § 1204(a) was inconsistent with the rule and therefore had to yield to Rule 101. The court transferred the appeal to the Pennsylvania Supreme Court because the trial court indirectly found § 1204(a) repugnant to the constitution by the judge's reliance on the reasoning of another case that found the statute unconstitutional. The court found that the Appellate Court Jurisdiction Act of 1970, 17 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 211.202 (1976), provided exclusive jurisdiction to the Supreme Court for this appeal.


OUTCOME: The court transferred the Commonwealth's appeal of an order that granted defendant's motion to suppress evidence to the Pennsylvania Supreme Court because the trial judge indirectly held that a state statute was repugnant to the Pennsylvania Constitution due to the fact that it was inconsistent with a rule of criminal procedure, and the Supreme Court was the exclusive jurisdiction for an appeal where a lower court found a law unconstitutional.

 

671 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Malone


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


244 Pa. Super. 62; 366 A.2d 584; 1976 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2127


March 8, 1976, Argued  
December 15, 1976, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 132 March Term, 1976

Appeal from the Memorandum and Order of the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County at No. 313 Miscellaneous Docket 1975.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County (Pennsylvania), which denied without a hearing his petition to have the police record of his arrest for solicitation to commit involuntary deviate sexual intercourse expunged and a return of his record from the FBI.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was arrested and charged with solicitation to commit involuntary deviate sexual intercourse. Police booked appellant according to normal procedure, and the charges were subsequently dismissed at the preliminary hearing. Appellant then filed a petition in which he sought to have to have the police record expunged and to have his record returned from the FBI. The lower court denied the petition on the ground that it did not have authority to grant the relief requested. The court maintained that the clear weight of authority held that one who was falsely or illegally arrested was entitled to expungement of his record and removal of his fingerprints from criminal files. The court stated that given the substantial interest of an accused in his good name and in freedom from the disability arising out of an arrest record, commonwealth was required to present compelling evidence to justify retention of such information. Accordingly the order was remanded for a hearing because the court held that the right to expungement existed under appropriate circumstances.


OUTCOME: The order denying appellant's petition was remanded for a hearing because the record did not show why charges were dismissed at the preliminary hearing and as such the court held that a determination as to whether appellant was entitled to expungement was required.

 

672 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Jones


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


246 Pa. Super. 346; 371 A.2d 874; 1976 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2227


June 16, 1975, Submitted  
December 15, 1976, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 928 October Term, 1975

Appeal from the Judgment of Sentence imposed March 5, 1975, in the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County, Trial Division, Criminal Section at Nos. 968/970 December term, 1969.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which denied his request for a new trial pursuant to a Post Conviction Hearing Act petition. Appellant contended that because a prior conviction, which was allegedly unconstitutional in violation of his right to appeal, had been used to impeach his credibility, he was entitled to a new trial.


OVERVIEW: After the trial court denied appellant's request for a new trial from his conviction of rape and robbery, appellant challenged the decision. The court affirmed, holding that there was no basis constitutionally to determine that the reliability of appellant's prior criminal conviction was suspect simply because an appellate court had not affirmed it on the merits. Therefore, although the general rule provided that use of convictions constitutionally invalid to impeach a defendant's credibility deprived him of due process, appellant had not shown that his rights to due process were violated. Appellant's prior criminal conviction was "unconstitutional" because appellant did not knowingly and voluntarily waive his right to appeal; then, because there was no trial transcript, a new trial was ordered and the commonwealth nol prossed it. Therefore, the general rule was wholly inappropriate in this case and the use of the prior conviction for impeachment purposes, did not require a new trial.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's denial of appellant's Post Conviction Hearing Act petition. Because appellant's rights to due process were not violated simply because an appellate court had not affirmed appellant's prior conviction on the merits, appellant was not entitled to a new trial where the unconstitutional prior conviction was used for impeachment purposes for a subsequent conviction.

 

673 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Adams


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


245 Pa. Super. 431; 369 A.2d 479; 1976 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2128


December 16, 1975, Submitted  
November 22, 1976, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 1471 October Term, 1975

Appeal from the Order of the Court of Common Pleas, Trial Division, Criminal Section of Philadelphia County at No. Misc. 75-00-1129.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a ruling from the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which convicted appellant of possession of offensive weapons in violation of 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 908, suspended appellant's sentence, and placed him on probation for a year.


OVERVIEW: An officer observed appellant with nunchaku sticks in his back pockets. The officer stopped appellant, seized the nunchaku sticks, and placed appellant under arrest for possessing a prohibited offensive weapon in violation of 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 908. At his municipal court trial, appellant testified that he was on his way to karate school. Subsequently, appellant was convicted of violating § 908 and placed on probation for a year. On appeal, the court vacated the sentence and discharged appellant. The court interpreted § 908 and found that there was no doubt that the nunchaku sticks could be used offensively to inflict serious injury. The court, however, found that a student such as appellant or an instructor of the martial arts would have occasion to use said sticks in a peaceful practice of karate exercises. In addition, the court found that many citizens of the commonwealth practiced the martial arts as a sport and an exercise. To eliminate the use, possession, and sale of items used in said sport simply because they could conceivably be used to inflict bodily injury was contrary to the express purpose of § 908.


OUTCOME: The court vacated appellant's sentence and discharged appellant. The court held that appellant was not in violation of statutory law by possessing a set of nunchaku sticks. The court found that it would be contrary to the express purpose of statutory law to eliminate the use, possession, and sale of items used in the sport of martial arts simply because they could conceivably be used to inflict bodily injury.

Mazzocone v. Willing


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


246 Pa. Super. 98; 369 A.2d 829; 1976 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2190


March 18, 1976, Argued  
November 22, 1976, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition Granted February 15, 1977.


PRIOR HISTORY: No. 475 October Term, 1976

Appeal from Decree dated November 10, 1975, Court of Common Pleas, Trial Division, Equity, Philadelphia County, at No. 4353 September Term, 1975.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant demonstrator sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas, Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which enjoined her from demonstrating against plaintiff attorneys and from publishing defamatory statements about plaintiffs.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff attorneys successfully prosecuted a workers' compensation claim on behalf of defendant demonstrator. Defendant developed the erroneous belief that plaintiffs diverted to themselves $ 25 of the fee for defendant's psychiatrist, who confirmed that he was fully paid. Defendant demonstrated against plaintiffs by pushing a shopping cart in front of their offices while ringing a cowbell and blowing a whistle and by wearing sign claiming plaintiffs stole her money. The trial court enjoined plaintiff from demonstrating against defendants or uttering defamatory statements against them. The court held that the traditional view that equity could not enjoin the publication of defamatory matter was not absolute and that application of the prohibition against prior restraint required an assessment of the particular circumstances. The court found that there was no public interest served by defendant's demonstrations. The court held that the trial court's injunction was too broad because it imposed sanctions beyond the scope of defendant's activities adjudged defamatory. Therefore, the court modified the injunction, and as modified, affirmed the judgment of the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court modified the trial court's injunction against plaintiff demonstrator to impose sanctions only on her activities that had been adjudged defamatory. As modified, the court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

 

675 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Kroger Co. v. O'Hara Township


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


243 Pa. Super. 479; 366 A.2d 254; 1976 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2998



November 16, 1976, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant corporations sought review of an order by the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania) which entered a final decree in equity dismissing appellants' complaint seeking an injunction to prohibit appellee townships from enforcing the Sunday Trading Laws, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. §7364.


OVERVIEW: Appellant corporations, which operated grocery stores, contended that the Sunday Trading Law, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. §7365, was being enforced against them in an unconstitutionally discriminatory manner. The court held that laws of this nature were constitutional. The court affirmed the final decree in equity because appellants failed to establish any pattern of discriminatory enforcement. In one situation, appellants and other supermarkets were prosecuted under the statute, but eleven other retail stores were also threatened with prosecution. The court found that another appellee township, which threatened eight supermarkets including appellants, were not acting discriminatorily since thirty-five other retail stores might be warned and the prosecutions might never take place. Furthermore, the court held that to prove that a conscious policy of enforcement was not justified in this case, appellants would have had to show that other retail stores that were not prosecuted did not fall into a class of exceptions listed under 18 Pa Cons. Stat. §7364(c). Appellants failed to show this discriminatory action.


OUTCOME: Trial court's final decree in equity denying appellants' request for injunction was affirmed because appellants failed to show that selectivity in the enforcement of a statute was not justified.

 

676 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Jones


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


242 Pa. Super. 471; 364 A.2d 368; 1976 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2065


March 8, 1976, Submitted  
September 27, 1976, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 11 March Term, 1976

Appeal from the judgment of sentence dated December 4, 1974, of the Court of Common Pleas of Franklin County, Criminal, at No. 13 of 1974.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Court of Common Pleas, Franklin County (Pennsylvania), which convicted him of driving under the influence of intoxicating liquor. Defendant contended that 75 P.S. § 624.1(h), which permitted his refusal to consent to a chemical test of his breath admitted into evidence as a factor to be considered in determining guilt or innocence, violative of his due process rights under U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed his conviction for driving under the influence of intoxicating liquor, contending that 75 P.S. § 624.1(h) violated his due process rights under U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The court affirmed the conviction, finding that the aforementioned statute, which permitted defendant's refusal to consent to a chemical test of his breath to be admitted into evidence as a factor to be considered in determining guilt or innocence, did not violate defendant's due process rights because the inference to be drawn from such evidence was not the sole element of appellant' s guilt, and, therefore, the inference did not have to prove him guilty beyond a reasonable doubt. The court concluded that there was direct evidence of defendant's guilt and the inference drawn from the refusal was just one piece of circumstantial evidence tending to prove the ultimate fact, and, under such circumstances, the inferred fact from the refusal did not have to follow beyond a reasonable doubt from the proved facts because the totality of the evidence established defendant's guilt beyond a reasonable doubt.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction of defendant for driving under the influence of intoxicating liquor, finding the admission into evidence of defendant's refusal to consent to a chemical test of his breath as a factor to be considered in determining guilt or innocence was not a violation of due process under the Fourteenth Amendment, because the inference to be drawn therefrom was not the sole element of defendant's guilt.

Marcus v. Diulus


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


242 Pa. Super. 151; 363 A.2d 1205; 1976 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2817


June 21, 1976, Argued  
September 27, 1976, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a business and its owners, filed a motion to quash an appeal brought by defendant police officials, which sought review of a decree of the equity division of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), enjoining defendants from using evidence seized from plaintiff business pursuant to a search warrant.


OVERVIEW: Defendant police officers and officials executed a search warrant on plaintiff business and discovered evidence of counterfeit goods and illegal transactions that occurred in another state. Plaintiff filed a declaratory action in equity court, requesting the equity court to find that the search and seizure conducted by defendants was illegal and to enjoin defendants from sending the evidence to officials in the other state. Defendants argued that the equity court lacked jurisdiction to enjoin a criminal proceeding. The trial court enjoined defendants from using the evidence and from sending the evidence to the other state. Defendants sought review. The court reversed, finding that the equity court lacked jurisdiction to enjoin criminal proceedings, because pursuant to Pa. R. Crim. P. 324, only the criminal courts had authority to prevent the use of unlawfully seized evidence.


OUTCOME: The motion to quash was denied, the decree was reversed, and the complaint in equity for injunctive relief and a declaratory judgment was dismissed for lack of jurisdiction. Plaintiffs, a business and its owners, were not entitled to injunctive relief to prevent the use of seized evidence, because the equity court lacked jurisdiction to enjoin the criminal proceeding.

Commonwealth v. Regan


No. 832, April T., 1975


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


240 Pa. Super. 635; 359 A.2d 403; 1976 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2015


November 25, 1975, Argued  
June 28, 1976, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Court of Common Pleas, Civil Division, of Allegheny County, No. 659 S.A. of 1975, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Mike Regan, a/k/a John Michael Regan.



DISPOSITION: Order reversed with a procedendo.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of an order from the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania), which denied appellant's petition for leave to file an appeal in forma pauperis. Appellant claimed that the conditioning of his appeal upon payment of a fine and costs amounted to a denial of due process and equal protection.


OVERVIEW: Appellant filed a petition for leave to appeal in forma pauperis. The trial court denied the petition because of an administrative order that required the payment of fines prior to the taking of an appeal from a summary conviction. On review, appellant contended that the conditioning of his appeal upon payment of a fine and costs amounted to a denial of due process and equal protection. The court agreed with this contention. The court found that the trial court improperly applied the administrative order requiring the payment of fines and costs as a condition to the appeal. The court determined that Pa. R. Crim. P. 67 did not require payment of costs or fines as a precondition to an appeal. Furthermore, the administrative order was not enforceable because it conflicted with Rule 67 by requiring proof of the payment of fines and costs to perfect an appeal. Lastly, the court reasoned that the payment of a fine as a condition precedent to an appeal was an unconstitutional denial of an appeal, therefore the court gave a construction to Rule 67 and the administrative order that was constitutional. Accordingly, the court reversed the order with a procedendo.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order that denied appellant's petition for leave to file an appeal in forma pauperis. The court found that the trial court improperly followed an administrative order that required the payment of fines and costs prior to an appeal because the applicable rule did not require payment, the administrative order conflicted with the applicable rule, and such payment amounted to a denial of due process and equal protection.

Commonwealth ex rel. Lattimore v. Gedney


No. 1318, Oct. T., 1975


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


240 Pa. Super. 226; 363 A.2d 786; 1976 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1951


September 12, 1975, Submitted  
April 22, 1976, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Court of Common Pleas, Trial Division, of Philadelphia, Nos. 74-06-0002 and 74-04-0084, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania ex rel. William Lattimore v. Dewaine Gedney, Director, Pre-Trial Services, Court Bail Program.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review after the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia (Pennsylvania) denied his petition for a writ of habeas corpus and granted an order of extradition.


OVERVIEW: While appellant was incarcerated on local charges, an arrest warrant charging appellant with being a fugitive from Tennessee was lodged as a detainer against him. The trial court held a preliminary arraignment, set bail at $ 1,000, and set a hearing. After the governor's warrant arrived, appellant filed a petition for writ of habeas corpus. While the preliminary identification was insufficient to support extradition (because of the confusion as to appellant's name), the court held that it justified commitment while awaiting proof that extradition was warranted.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order that denied his petition for a writ of habeas corpus and granted an order of extradition. The court held that the magistrate in the asylum need make a finding of probable cause.

Commonwealth v. Duncan

Superior Court of Pennsylvania


239 Pa. Super. 539; 363 A.2d 803; 1976 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1901

PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from judgment of sentence of Court of Common Pleas, Trial Division, of Philadelphia, Nos. 74-00-2624 and 74-05-382, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Darryl Duncan.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a judgment of sentence of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia (Pennsylvania), which was imposed following appellant's conviction of harassment under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2709.


OVERVIEW: The trial court convicted appellant of harassing, annoying, or alarming another person under 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2709. The court further found that appellant's speaking constituted a course of conduct prohibited by the statute and that appellant's intent to commit the criminal act could have been inferred from his words or actions when viewed in light of the attendant circumstances. Thus, the trial court was justified in inferring from appellant's repeated requests and rebuttals that appellant was acting deliberately and with the intention of annoying the prosecutrix.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of sentence imposed upon appellant following his conviction of harassing, annoying, or alarming another person because appellant's lewd speech was not constitutionally protected free speech. Appellant's repeated lewd speech constituted a course of conduct within the meaning of the statute prohibiting harassment.

 

682 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth ex rel. Yentzer v. Carpenter


No. 769, Oct. T., 1975


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


240 Pa. Super. 202; 362 A.2d 1101; 1976 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1933


September 9, 1975, Submitted  
March 29, 1976, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Court of Common Pleas of Potter County, No. 11 of 1974, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania ex rel. Jennylou Yentzer v. Gary Carpenter.



DISPOSITION: Order reversed and appellant discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant putative father sought review of the judgment of the Court of Common Pleas of Potter County (Pennsylvania), which instructed the jury that paternity need only be proved by a preponderance of the evidence in appellee mother's action for support of an illegitimate child under the Civil Procedural Support Law, 62 P.S. § 2043.31 et seq. Appellant denied paternity and asked for a "reasonable doubt" charge.


OVERVIEW: Appellee mother brought an action for support of an illegitimate child under the Civil Procedural Support Law, 62 P.S. § 2043.31 et seq., in which she named appellant putative father as the father. Appellant denied paternity and presumably requested that he be tried by a jury. At trial appellant asked the trial court to charge that appellee had the burden of proving paternity beyond a reasonable doubt. The trial court instructed the jury that paternity had to be proved only by a preponderance of the evidence. Appellant contended that the charge was erroneous. The court reversed the order and ordered that appellant be discharged because the trial court lacked subject matter jurisdiction. The court held that the lack of subject matter jurisdiction could never be waived and could be raised at any stage in the proceedings by the parties or by the court on its own motion. The court held that a criminal proceeding on the issue of paternity, 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 4323, was mandatory absent an express waiver by appellant. Appellee proceeded civilly, and appellant demanded a jury trial on the issue of paternity. Therefore, the civil court was without jurisdiction to proceed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order and ordered that appellant putative father be discharged because a civil trial court did not have subject matter jurisdiction to decide the question of paternity and a criminal proceeding on the issue of paternity was mandatory absent an express waiver by appellant. Lack of subject matter jurisdiction could not be waived.

Commonwealth v. Wadzinski


No. 734, Oct. T., 1975


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


239 Pa. Super. 76; 361 A.2d 790; 1976 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1971


June 12, 1975, Submitted  
March 29, 1976, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Court of Common Pleas of Luzerne County, No. 9390 of 1973, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Frank J. Wadzinski.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Luzerne County (Pennsylvania), which denied relief upon appellant's petition for certiorari to that court from a judgment of a magistrate that appellant was guilty of making a political announcement in violation of 25 P. S. § 3234. Appellant contended that the magistrate violated. Pa. R. Crim. P. 63(e) by not announcing the verdict immediately upon the conclusion of the trial.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was found guilty by a magistrate of making a political announcement in violation of 25 P. S. § 3234. The court of common pleas entered an order that denied appellant's petition for certiorari. The court affirmed the order. The court held that there was no showing of any prejudice to appellant by the announcement of the verdict and sentence by certified mail two days after the hearing rather than in open court. The court also held that the record refuted any implication that the magistrate regarded the case as a civil case instead of a criminal one and that the magistrate applied the burden of proof of a preponderance of the evidence instead of beyond a reasonable doubt. The court stated that the complaint was clearly labelled "criminal complaint." The court also stated that certification by the magistrate was that there was probable cause for the issuance of process. The court noted that the transcript made up by the magistrate was conspicuously labelled "summary conviction." The court concluded that the finding of the magistrate of a verdict of guilty indicated a clear understanding that he was dealing with a criminal case.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the court of common pleas, which denied appellant's petition for certiorari to that court from a judgment that appellant was guilty of making a political announcement in violation of 25 P. S. § 3234. The court held that there was no showing of prejudice to appellant by the magistrate not announcing the verdict immediately upon the conclusion of the trial.

Commonwealth v. Bryant


No. 541, Oct. T., 1975


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


239 Pa. Super. 43; 361 A.2d 350; 1976 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2093


September 12, 1975, Submitted  
March 29, 1976, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from judgment of sentence of Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County, July T., 1974, No. 2537, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Robert Bryant.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Court of Common Pleas of Montgomery County (Pennsylvania), rendered a judgment of life imprisonment against defendant for simple assault and assault by a life prisoner. Defendant contended that the state failed to prove the essential elements of 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2704 and life imprisonment for a simple assault constituted cruel and unusual punishment prohibited by U.S. Const. amend. VIII and Pa. Const. art. I, § 13.


OVERVIEW: On the way to the hole, defendant struck one of the guards on the back of the neck, and the guard's injuries caused the guard to miss work for a three hours on one day and the greater part of the next day. Defendant was convicted of simple assault and assault by a life prisoner, and sentenced to life imprisonment as mandated by 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2704. Defendant contended that the state failed to prove every element of 18 Pa. Cons. Stat. § 2704; however, the court found the testimony of the struck guard and another guard to be sufficient. Defendant also contended that § 2704 punishment of life imprisonment for a simple assault committed by a prisoner constituted cruel and unusual punishment prohibited by U.S. Const. amend. VIII and Pa. Const. art. I, § 13. The court affirmed the conviction, holding that the fixing of penalties for crimes was a legislative function, and it was a maxim of constitutional law that the legislature will be presumed to have acted within constitutional limits. The court concluded that an appellate court should not declare an act of assembly void unless its violation of the constitution was so clear as to preclude doubt or hesitation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction of life imprisonment for simple assault by a prisoner, holding that the statute fixing such punishment was constitutional and not cruel and unusual punishment, and that the state had proved every element of its case.

Commonwealth v. Smith


No. 503, Oct. T., 1975


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


239 Pa. Super. 440; 361 A.2d 881; 1976 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2113


December 9, 1975, Argued  
March 29, 1976, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from order of Court of Common Pleas of Chester County, May T., 1972, No. 209, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. James F. Smith.



DISPOSITION: Case remanded and court below directed to modify its order.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the order from the Court of Common Pleas of Chester County (Pennsylvania) requiring defendant to pay the entire cost of the prosecution for a felony for which he was acquitted. Defendant was, however, convicted of the misdemeanor crime of illegal possession of a firearm.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was tried by a jury on three bills of indictment charging defendant with murder, possession of a firearm without a license, and two counts of pointing and discharging a deadly weapon. The jury found defendant guilty of one count of illegal possession of a firearm and acquitted him of all the remaining charges. Defendant was sentenced to a term of imprisonment of 18 to 36 months and to pay the costs of prosecution. After defendant served the minimum prison term and was released, he petitioned the court to remit any amount already paid in excess of his determined cost to prosecute. The court reversed the trial court's order denying defendant's petition and remanded the case to the lower court to determine what portion of the costs, if any, were necessary to try defendant on those charges which resulted in acquittal, and to modify its order imposing costs in accordance with the findings. The court held that defendant could not be ordered to pay the costs of prosecuting the felony charge because he was acquitted, and thus could only be charged the portion of the costs necessary to convict him on the misdemeanor charge.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order requiring defendant to pay the entire cost of the prosecution of a felony for which he was acquitted. The court remanded the cause to the lower court to determine what portion of the costs, if any, were necessary to try defendant on those charges for which he was acquitted, and to modify its order imposing costs in accordance with its findings.

 

687 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Bliss


No. 63, March T., 1975


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


239 Pa. Super. 347; 362 A.2d 365; 1976 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2118


March 10, 1975, Submitted  
March 29, 1976, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from judgment of sentence of Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County, Nos. 1648, 1649, 1709, 1710, 1711, and 1754 of 1973, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. William E. Bliss.



DISPOSITION: Appeal quashed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged his conviction in the Court of Common Pleas of Dauphin County (Pennsylvania) for multiple sex crimes and kidnapping.


OVERVIEW: Following appellant's conviction for various sex offenses and kidnapping, he did not file post-trial motions, but he did file pro se a post-conviction petition under the Post Conviction Hearing Act, 19 P.S. § 1180-1 et seq., (1975), which the trial court denied without appointing counsel and without a hearing. The court quashed his appeal and found that trial counsel was incompetent for not preserving appellant's issues for review by failing to file post-trial motions. The court found that appellate counsel was incompetent by not requesting a remand of the record for an evidentiary hearing on whether appellant voluntarily and intelligently waived his right to file post-trial motions. The court found that, because no post-trial motions were filed, whether the trial court erred in denying appellant's petition without appointing counsel and without a hearing was the only appealable issue and that appellant's counsel brief did not address it. Appellant's brief addressed only the kidnapping conviction and sentence and it was filed too late to be timely with regard to those issues. The court suggested that the trial court appoint new counsel to inquire further into the matters.


OUTCOME: The court quashed the appeal, as both trial and appellate counsel were incompetent, and suggested that the trial court determine if further proceedings were warranted and appoint new counsel. Trial counsel's failure to file post-trial motions denied appellant post-conviction review and appellate counsel failed to address the only appealable issue of whether it was error to dismiss appellant's uncounseled petition for a post-conviction hearing.

 

688 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Cost


No. 117, April T., 1975


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


238 Pa. Super. 591; 362 A.2d 1027; 1976 Pa. Super. LEXIS 2246


April 17, 1975, Argued  
March 29, 1976, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from judgment of sentence of Court of Common Pleas of Greene County, No. 30 of 1974, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. Joseph Cost.



DISPOSITION: Appellant's conviction for burglary reversed and judgment of sentence on robbery conviction affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Greene County (Pennsylvania), which found him guilty of robbery and burglary and imposed punishment. Defendant claimed that there was insufficient evidence to support a conviction for robbery and that it was error to admit his post-arrest statements because he was intoxicated, did not understand his Miranda rights, and did not sign the waiver of rights form.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of burglary and robbery following a jury trial, and was sentenced to jail. Defendant appealed. The court found that the trial judge's charge on burglary was clearly erroneous because it permitted the jury to reach a finding of guilt based solely upon the defendant's intent at the time of entry and ignored the limiting phrase of the statute, "unless the premises are at the time open to the public." The court found defendant's statements were voluntary, that the trial court properly denied defendant's motion to suppress, that the presence of the three adverse witnesses did not result in coercing defendant into giving his oral statements. The court reversed the conviction for burglary and affirmed the conviction for robbery.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction for burglary was reversed because the jury charge was clearly erroneous in that it permitted them to find guilt based solely upon the defendant's intent at the time of entry and ignored the limiting phrase of the statute, and the judgment of sentence for the robbery conviction was affirmed because defendant's statements were knowingly and voluntarily made.

 

689 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Krasner


Nos. 1470 and 1471, Oct. T., 1974


Superior Court of Pennsylvania


238 Pa. Super. 187; 357 A.2d 558; 1976 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1611


March 18, 1975, Argued  
February 2, 1976, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals from judgment of sentence of Court of Common Pleas of Chester County, Jan. T., 1972, Nos. 34, 36, and 39, in case of Commonwealth of Pennsylvania v. John Krasner and Robert Pine.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of sentence reversed and defendants discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed their convictions by the Court of Common Pleas of Chester County (Pennsylvania), for selling obscene literature in violation of 18 P.S. § 4524. Jury verdicts were returned against both defendants convicting them as charged. The sole issue appealed was whether § 4524 was unconstitutional in light of decisions from the United States Supreme Court and subsequent decisions of the Pennsylvania courts dealing with obscenity.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were convicted of selling obscene literature in violation of 18 P.S. § 4524. The jury returned verdicts against both defendants convicting them of the offense as charged. The sole issue defendants addressed to the court on appeal was whether § 4524 passed constitutional muster in light of the test for state regulation of obscenity announced in 1973 by the United States Supreme Court. The court held that under that standard, it was apparent that the statute was constitutionally deficient because it did not specifically define the sexual conduct that it purported to proscribe. This court noted the importance of this element, because without it an individual is effectively deprived of notice as to what type of depictions in his literature will result in the imposition of criminal sanctions. The court also cited a recent decision in which it held that § 4524 was unconstitutional because it failed to specifically defined the type of conduct it purported to describe. Because the statute under which defendants were convicted did not comply with constitutional requirements, the court held that their convictions had to be reversed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of sentence and ordered defendants discharged. Because the statute under which the defendants were convicted did not meet constitutional requirements, the court held that the convictions had to be reversed. The statute under which defendants were charged failed to specifically define the sexual conduct it purported to proscribe, in violation of the constitutional standard adopted by the United States Supreme Court.

 

690 of 1476 DOCUMENTS


Lanzetta v. Commonwealth, Pennsylvania Bd. of Probation & Parole


No. 1131 C.D. 1989


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


130 Pa. Commw. 312; 568 A.2d 283; 1989 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 819


October 20, 1989, Submitted on Briefs  
December 28, 1989, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] PETITION FOR REVIEW (§ 763).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner parolee sought review of the decision of respondent Board of Probation and Parole (Pennsylvania), which denied petitioner's request for administrative relief and recommitted petitioner to serve his remaining backtime for violating three technical conditions of his parole.


OVERVIEW: After petitioner parolee was charged with simple assault and public intoxication, respondent Pennsylvania Board of Probation and Parole, issued a warrant to commit and detain petitioner for violating his parole. The warrant failed to set forth the specific parole violations. After pleading guilty to the lesser offense of disorderly conduct, petitioner was given written notice of his alleged parole violations. After a violation hearing, respondent recommitted petitioner to serve his backtime, based on petitioner's disorderly conduct, assaultive behavior, and his consumption of alcohol, and denied a request for administrative relief. Petitioner sought review and the court affirmed. Respondent was not required to hold a preliminary hearing when petitioner had already been detained on the criminal charges, under Pa. Code § 71.2. In addition, petitioner's constitutional rights were not violated when he was given a full opportunity to litigate the alleged violations at his violation hearing. Also, the disorderly conduct and assault violations did not involve the same act and did not merge. Further, the court found no due process violations.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of respondent Pennsylvania Board of Probation and Parole to recommit petitioner parolee to serve his remaining sentence. Respondent did not have to hold a preliminary hearing after issuing a warrant charging petitioner with violating his parole because petitioner was already in custody on pending criminal charges.

Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania

PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] PETITION FOR REVIEW (IN THE NATURE OF A COMPLAINT FOR DECLARATORY JUDGMENT AND INJUNCTIVE RELIEF) WITH NOTICE TO PLEAD.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Department of Environmental Resources filed a petition for review, an application for special relief, and a motion for expedited consideration of the application for special relief with the Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania involving the constitutionality of the Regulatory Review Act, 71 PA. Stat. §§ 745.1 -- 745.15, in opposition to respondent, the Pennsylvania Senate.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner Department of Environmental Resources sought an application for special relief to determine the constitutionality of the Regulatory Review Act (the Act), 71 PA. Stat. §§ 745.1 -- 745.15, in opposition to respondent, the Pennsylvania Senate. The court had to determine whether the Act violated the constitutional doctrine of separation of powers between and among the branches of the commonwealth's government. The court found that although § 2 of the Act contained a disclaimer that the Act was not intended to create rights or benefits, it was apparent that the Independent Regulatory Review Commission's (IRRC) action in blocking the required publication of a regulation was an impediment to the executive's rule-making authority inherent in its duty to administer the laws. The court held that petitioner met its burden of proving that the Act violated the separation of powers doctrine. Accordingly, the court granted petitioner's motion for summary judgment and held §§ 6(b) and 7(b) of the Act unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court granted the motion for summary judgment by petitioner Department of Environmental Resources and held §§ 6(b) and 7(b) of the Regulatory Review Act unconstitutional in their petition for review and application for special relief involving the constitutionality of the Act. The court held that petitioner met its burden of proving that the Act violated the separation of powers doctrine.

Buck v. Commonwealth, Dep't of Public Welfare


No. 2954 C.D. 1987


Commonwealth Court of Pennsylvania


130 Pa. Commw. 51; 566 A.2d 1269; 1989 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 764


September 11, 1989, Argued  
December 1, 1989, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from PETITION FOR REVIEW.



DISPOSITION: Having found no error of law committed nor any violation of constitutional rights, the final order of the Secretary of DPW is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner recipient sought review of a final order of respondent Department of Public Welfare, which affirmed a determination by the office of hearings and appeals that petitioner's entire pension was available to pay for his nursing home care.


OVERVIEW: After initially authorizing medical assistance nursing home care benefits for petitioner recipient, the Philadelphia County Assistance Office notified petitioner of its intention to increase his patient pay amount because it had considered only one-half, rather than all, of petitioner's pension. Respondent Department of Public Welfare affirmed a determination by the office of hearings and appeals that petitioner's entire pension was available to pay for his nursing home care, and petitioner sought review. The court affirmed. The court held that respondent neither erred nor acted arbitrarily without statutory support when it applied the name-on-the-instrument rule to determine ownership of petitioner's pension. The court held also that application of the rule did not amount to an infringement of a right to marital privacy, and that the rule did not employ an impermissible irrebuttable presumption that operated to deny due process of law.


OUTCOME: Respondent Department of Public Welfare's secretary's order that affirmed a determination that petitioner recipient's entire pension was available to pay for his nursing home care was affirmed. Respondent's application of the name-on-the-instrument rule to determine ownership of income was neither erroneous nor without statutory support, did not violate a right of marital privacy, and did not deny due process of law.
